O sons, since I have completed this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, through its fulfillment in me, and through its expositions and declarations, it has become clear to me that you will understand this treatise, which is a part of the operation with the hand, i.e., surgery.
For the operation of the hand is nearly unknown in our realm and time, having been deprived of it almost entirely, while those who know something about it have distorted its science, and its procedures are obscured; only a few small descriptions of it remain in the books of the Ancients, which confuse the matter and lead to error and uncertainty, so that the understanding of it is blocked and its force and art diminished.
And it has seemed good to me that I should animate this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that it might come with the forms of the tools of cautery and other instruments of the work as they are through the addition of the declaration, and for its preparation, which is necessary.
But the reason why no one is adept with their hand in our time is because the art of medicine is long, and its practitioner must be exercised in the science of anatomy, which knows the parts of the members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and has knowledge of the bones, the nerves, and the tendons, and the number of them, and their movement; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and their places of emission.
And so Hypocrites says that though his name is small, in the work he is great, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said about anatomy, it has been made clear that he should not fall into error, by which men will die, as I have seen many of them, for some are formed in this science and yet stray from it without experience.
For I saw an experienced fool of a doctor pierce a scrophula on a woman's neck, and pierce some arteries in the neck so that much blood flowed and the woman died under his hand.
And I saw another doctor who pulled out a stone from a man who was in great pain, and the stone was large; he came to him and extracted that stone with a fragment of the bladder, so that the man died on the third day.
And I called for them to stop extracting it, and saw that due to the size of the stone and the condition of the patient, I knew that he would die.
And I saw another doctor who received a stipend from a duke of our land for surgery, and he treated a black slave who had a fracture in his thigh; and preparing with his ignorance, he extracted the fracture over the plague with powders and splints with strong compression, and did not let the plague breathe.
And he healed according to his desires, and he bound it, and let it be for several days, and commanded that I should not loosen the bandage; and so it stayed as it was, and the thigh and the leg became pustular, and it came to ruin.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to loosen the bandage; and after there was tranquility and he was eased of his pains.
But indeed corruption was confirmed in the member, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption wandered in the member until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who perforated a cancerous ulcer, and it festered for some days, until the wickedness of it increased with its lord.
The reason for this is that the black humor is pure in its melancholy, therefore it does not come to it with fire at all, but rather that it is in a member which suffers from everything that is hostile to it.
And so, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation of the hand should be understood in two ways: one is operation which is associated with health, and the other is work with which the greatest dispositions are endangered.
And I have already exhorted this in every place of this book where there is error and fear; so it is necessary for you to guard against this and leave that, so that fools do not hinder the way of speaking and disgracing you.
And therefore take up your weapons with zeal and with the protection of God, and your diseases with facility and with firmness.
And use the better way by the grace of God so that health and laudable success may result, and put aside terrible diseases of difficult cure, and wield your weapons against what makes you fearful, so that idleness in your faith and in your name does not leave you: for it remains to your glory, and more in the world and beyond to your sanctities.
Gaulias already said in some of his maxims: "Do not treat ill diseases ill, so that bad doctors are not named."
And I have composed this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered on the head, in the forms of the instruments and the tools of cauteries, and of what is necessary in operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of bleeding and piercing, and of cupping, and of plasters, and of extraction of thorns and like things, all chaptered and ordered in the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and of dislocation, and of cure of torque and like things; a chapter ordered on the head, in the forms of the instruments.
I shall say what the quality is of the ingredients of that cautery and of the things in it, and in what condition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the speech of the quality of the ingredient of cautery and of the thing in it is long and prolix, and subtle by science, and secret hidden; and much has been spoken about it by many wise men, and from it they have disagreed, and for me a little of their speeches is enough, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery confers universally on all complections, save two complektions which are caustic complektions without material.
In the caustic and dry complection with material they disagreed; for some said that cautery is beneficial for it, and others said the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in disease which is by quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and what is convenient is that disease caustic and dry be healed with what is cauted and dry.
And he said the contrary of that, that by cautery is made remedy in all disease caustic and dry which is seen in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its humidities to the complection of fire, you rub the body of man.
And I say by the speech of that, that experiment that has been discovered to me several times.
But it is not necessary to come upon this anyone who has already been demonstrated and exercised in the chapter of cauterization by former exercise, and has known the diversity of the complexions of men and the disposition of diseases in them, and their causes, and their accidents, and the space of time of them.
But in the other complexions there is no need for fear, especially in damp and moist diseases, for all doctors have agreed on these, and they do not disagree about using cauterization to treat them.
And understand, sons, that the secret of curing by cauterization with fire and its goodness is that it is a simple substance whose action does not extend to the members joined to the one cauterized, and it does not affect any other member except with a slight injury.
But the action of burning medicine extends to other members, and it strongly brings about a difficult-to-cure disease in them, and strongly weakens them.
But fire, by its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, does not do that except to cause it to superfluous. And we have known through experience and through long service in studying the truth of causes, and so I am excused from the length of speech. And if it were not for my not being free to read this hidden secret to you with fire, and the quality of its operation on bodies and its expulsion through which you expel diseases, by philosophical speech demonstrated, which would be acute in your ears.
And understand, sons, that they disagree on the time when it is convenient to do cauterization, and they pause at the best time in spring.
And I say that cauterization is convenient at all times, as long as the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of what is drawn by cauterization, and especially if the cauterization is for pain or necessary pains, strong and violent, which do not tolerate delay for fear of evil, which is greater than slight harm coming through the weather.
And know this, or, sons, that which is not believed except by the common people and foolish doctors, it is that to that disease which is healed by cauterization there is no reduction at all time, and they pause at the consequent one.
And the cause is not as they think through that, for cauterization is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries up the moistnesses which are the cause of the pains coming.
But cauterization surpasses medicine through willingness in its strength and virtue of its operation, and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that his disease will return at some time of the year, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of the disease, and its strength, and what has been prepared in his body through the gathering of superfluities in him, and what he has received from him in food and likeness from those causes; but that disease for which cauterization is administered is a subtle disease in members with few superfluities and moistnesses, thus as cauterization for toothache and its like to it, it is possible that this pain will not return in him, but what happens in small amounts.
And the common speech is that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, and it is a true speech, not that they understand in it, for they believe that there is no cure that benefits from medicine or anything else except cauterization.
And the cause is contrary to that; and the intention that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer the methods of curing in certain diseases, and those medicines do not confer it, and then at the end of the cause, we administer hom cautery, and it benefits from that. And from here we see that cauterization is the last remedy of medicine, not according to the intention which the common people and foolish doctors understand.
And they say that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron, and they do not say this except for the equality of gold and subtlety of its substance.
And they say that the place of cauterization does not matter, even though it is absolutely true, for I have seen and proven that it only happens in some bodies without the others, and cauterization with it is good and better than with iron as they say; but when you do cauterization with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, for it is heated according to the quantity that you want through the roughness of the gold, and prepares to receive heat; and if you put on fire some leaves so that the craftsman sees through the occupation of that.
And for this, cauterization with iron is done to us more lightly and closer to the correctness of operation.
And I have arranged this chapter on cauterization according to chapters, and have arranged those of the head among the feet, so that they may ask him for what they want from him.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers with moistness and coldness the superficial accident of which it has two causes, it is caused by soda and multitude of fluxes, called corissa, from the parts of the ears and nostrils flowing, and multitude of sleep, and toothache and of the gullet, and universally of all disease that comes through coldness, thus with paralysis, stroke, and its like in those diseases.
The form of this cauterization is as follows: first of all, order the patient to be evacuated by laxative medicine three or four times, depending on the strength of the patient and his age and habit.
And then command that he washes his head with water, and then let him sit between your knees on a low bench, and place his hands over his feet.
And then pause the edge of your palm close to the tip of the nose between your eyebrows. But if after three days it has not arrived, mark that location with ink, and then light the cauterization olive, of which this is the form:
And then place it over the location marked by ink, in such a way that it descends, and your hand be forced little by little, and you turn it.
And then lift your hand lightly and look at the location, and if you see some amount of the head, thus as the amount of grains of sand, then lift your hand again, and if it does not return to your hand with that iron, with another one, if it is cold, between what has been seen by you that I have said to you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the location and leave it for three days.
And then place on it wet cotton in vinegar and let it sit there until the fire ash is destroyed. And then heal the location with tetraceutical ointment once it has healed.
And they say that when the Roman plaster is open, as many miles are added to the distance, for it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the head be cauterized within the eye socket and have the cauterization within the eye socket as it is attached to some part of the brain.
And then it is cut that which is attached to the bone, and then it is healed.
Others say that with the cautery they come to the end, while they press on the strong bone as they enter it, and this is the form of small Alfecat. And they consider that from that location they expel the vapor from the head.
And if the wound is open for a long time, it should be treated until it is healed and consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in some men according to the way of fear; and leaving that aside, for me it is better for health when there is no pain, since the head is relieved through solution of continuity as we have testified in other members, and especially when the nature of the patient's head is of a phlegmatic nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better for me, and it is used and works with health.
Chapter second.
Of cautery of the head.
When in all the head there occurs chronic and long-lasting pain and this is prolonged in the patient, using serots and clysters and head-purges and plasters and poultices and bandages and herbs and similar things, and especially if it has already been cauterized with a cautery which we count and does not benefit him from any of those causes, then consider that if the nature of the patient's head is of a strong creation in its nature; and it is phlegmatic and finds great heat in it, then cauterize it with another cautery after that one. Then cauterize it on the crown of his head with a cautery so that the vein is cut. The vein is cut and the quantity which we have counted is revealed and cauterized with a cautery in the back of the head in the place which is called the vessel — and it is that place where a leech is applied. And you put your hand on it, and do not reveal the bone, for the patient will find much greater pain from that than from all the other cauteries of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, for the cautery with which you cautarize the horns of the head and the occiput is more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is how it is done.
Chapter third.
Of cautery of recent and not antique emigration.
When in the front of the head there is painful swelling, or pain extends to the eye, the head should be evacuated with cleansing medicines, and other treatment should be administered, which I have described in the chapters on diseases; and if all does not help, cautery in it is in two ways: either cautery with an acute burning medicine, or with a hot iron.
But cautery with a burning medicine is that you take a piece of wool and char it, and scrape away its edges and bind the ends of it on both sides of the head. Then cut the place of pain in the temple with a pointed lance so that it is made under the vein and hidden.
Then tighten it with a tight bandage, leaving that aside for the space of three hours; then loosen it and treat the ash, leaving the wound for two or three days, and then put cold water on it until the place becomes swollen.
Then heal it with ointment as it heals.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written in the two treaties of burning medicines.
But the cautery with an iron is done according to this way: heat for cautery, of which this is the form:
and it is called a closed key, for the head of it is thus shaped like a key in which there is some curvature, and in the middle place of it there is a small eminence.
Then pause over the place of pain, and hold your hand — so that it is not burnt by the iron which is below — and move the hot iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of it that is burnt by the heat of the vein thus according to its nature and size, and raise your hand so that the artery below is not burnt, so that there comes no flow of blood.
Then soak a bandage in salt water and pause it over the place, leaving that for three days; then pause the bandage moistened with wine, and heal with ointment as it heals.
And if you wish, cauterize with the end of the scalpel according to cautery, and beware of incision of artery in this recent and not antique emigration.
Chapter fourth.
Of cautery of ancient emigration.
When you treat emigration with what we have said of the preceding treatment, and what we have said in the chapters on diseases, and the treatment does not help, and you see that the disease is not satisfied by it according to what we have said of the first cautery with medicine or of cautery with fire, then when you ignite the cautery-scalpel so that it is whitened, push it onto the place of pain with a line of length according to its size, or as much as that; and raise your hand by a pathway and tighten it so that you cut the artery and it comes into the bone.
However it is necessary for you to beware of the continuation of the mandible which is gnawed by chewing, and that the ligament not be burnt and the nerve which gnaws that, and that no spasms come.
And if it is upon the custody and caution of flow of blood from the artery which is cut, for in the cutting of it there is danger, and especially to him who ignores what he does and is not experienced and educated; leave the operation at the beginning.
And I will come with remembrance of the rule of flow of blood from the artery according to his own way, in his own place of this free book.
And if you see of this disease that it does not help that cautery and you see the body of the patient tolerable, cauterize him in the middle place of the head as we have counted, and heal the wound as it heals.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter on stretching of artery with cautery having two edges, and this cautery is better and more potent.
Chapter fifth.
Of cautery for pains of ears.
When in the ear there is pain from heat and it is treated with lytic causes and with other treatment which I have said in the division, and the pain is not relieved, then ignite the cautery and this is called puncta, of which this is the form:
Then push the point of ignition and it is carried into the whole ear as it is turned, or in the circuit of whichever part if the pain is in whichever part. Take it away with the cautery of the edge of the ear a little by little as you test the place with touch.
And if the cautery of ten points in all the ears, and whichever part, then heal the places as they are healed.
Chapter sixth.
Of cautery for tetter.
Tetter which is healed and cautery of it is not but of the spices which come through phlegm, according to what we have said in the chapters on diseases.
Beware of cautery of the species that comes through drying or through spasm of the nerve.
When you treat this type of torture with chains and whipings and threats, and your cure does not work, then you should cauterize the patient with three cauteries: you must apply the first one at the root of the ear, the second one below his temple, and the third one at the junction of two lips.
And apply your cautery on the opposite side of the affected area, because softening only occurs in the part that appears healthy.
And the way to perform cauterization is to cauterize the patient on the right side of the upper part of the ear, a little above the horn of the head, and another one on the temple, the length of which should be according to the length of the finger toe, and place your hand with the cautery until half of the amount of the thickness of the skin is burned.
And this is the form of cauterization, and it is the specialty of the surgeon, the shape of his tool being like a table. But in fact, it is more subtle: when you see that there is too much thickness, you should cut a little bit into the patient.
Then treat the wound with what is called the direct process until it heals.
Chapter 7.
On cauterization for old apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you treat it with what we have said and your medicine does not work, and the patient does not have fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on both horns of his head, as we have said; and with a cautery in the middle part of the head, as we have said; and with a cautery in the neck. And the manner of cauterization is done according to what has been said above.
And it is cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and it should be treated with what has already been said.
Chapter 8.
On cauterization for oblivion caused by phlegm.
Give the patient at the beginning large whips and purifying pills.
Then scrape the whole head of the patient and place on his neck a plaster made from cedar resin, as described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply this several times, because this is the way to cauterize. And do that according to the order of medicines which we have already said.
And if he gets better with that, good; and if not, then do to him three cauteries on the back part of his head, which descend from the upper part of the head to the lower part of the neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and treat the cauteries with what has been said.
But if you think that he can bear it and the patient can tolerate it, then do to him a cautery in the middle part; and if you see greater tolerance, then cauterize him on both horns.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form given above.
Chapter 9.
On cauterization for paraplegia and on softening all the body.
Give the patient whips and purifying pills in the head as we have said; then scrape his head and cauterize it with a cautery in the middle part of the head, and with a cautery on both horns of the head, and with a cautery on the back part of him, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of the neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body and the patient can tolerate it, and the disease is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of the back, and lastly with a cautery until half of the thickness of the skin is burned, and raise your hand. Then treat that until it is healed, and let this be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter 10.
On cauterization for epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And so give the brain of him at the beginning whips and the other treatment which we have said in the division when the patient is large and can tolerate the exception of medicines.
And if the patient is a child who does not suffer from medicines, then administer healing and masticables, before which for several days there should be benefit from food.
Then scrape the head of the patient, and cauterize him with a cautery in the middle part of the head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery on his occipital bone, and on each horn of his head. And if the patient is strong and can tolerate it, then cauterize him with cauteries as we have said, having paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped according to what has been said before.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a subtle cautery according to this manner.
Chapter 11.
On cauterization for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humors and phlegm, then cauterize him with cauteries as we have said in having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is excessive decline into melancholy, and the body of the patient is humid, then give him what purifies his brain according to what has preceded in the division.
Then scrape the head of the patient, and properly make a torch made of lye over the circle, and then place it in the middle part of the head.
And let the patient be for sixty days, and let him be held from every side.
Then give him a lining; and it is more worthy to make an old sheep’s udder. And warm that over the fire with moderate heat according to measure, as long as he can tolerate it when it is on him. Then place that in the middle part of his head over the circle, and rub it until it is warm.
And do that with the patient once a week for a month, and let him have his other regime as long as he is better; or if you want, cauterize him making small points according to his age, unless you hold your hand with a cautery in this way: this is a special cautery which moistens the brain equally.
Then give that cut in a bag made of wool or in a towel of flannel.
Chapter 12.
On cauterization for water descending into the navel.
When you see the beginning of water in the navel because of phlegm as we have said in the division, then begin and give the patient what purifies his head, and guard him from all humors.
And make that sweat in a bath made of vinegar for several days.
Then command that he wash his head, and cauterize him with a cautery in the middle part of the head; and then cauterize him with two cauteries on two temples if the beginning is of water in two navels, or on one side if it is the beginning of water in one navel.
Penetrate with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin; and let the cauteries be long in that length of the temples.
Guard against flows of blood when it is cauterized; and if you see flows of blood from him, stop them with some medicine which is possible for you.
I bring wisdom, which comes from the extraction of arrows and from the cauterization of these wounds and the flow of blood.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the head, under the bones, with two cauteries made recently.
Chapter 13.
On the cauterization of old tears.
When tears from the holes are old and persistent, and flow through veins and arteries in the head, causing a surplus of phlegmatic moisture, then cauterize it with the cautery we described in the chapter on the beginning of water gushing from the holes; apply the cautery in the middle part of the head, and use two cauteries on the neck below the temples, and two cauteries on the neck under the bones. And if there is a need for more cauterization, then cauterize it all over the root of the hole on the upper lip with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
On the cauterization for the odor of the nose.
When you treat a patient with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not work, then prepare and give the patient enemas for three nights; then shave his head, and cauterize the midline of the head with an olive-oil cautery. Then cauterize the nose with a small cautery at two cauteries above the upper lip under the hair.
And be careful not to burn the artery.
This is the form of the nail cautery.
Chapter 15.
On the cautery for softening the eyelids of the holes.
When the eyelid of the hole is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with one cautery using this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you are cauterizing it above the two eyebrows, do so with two small cauteries on each side, and have them extended from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand too hard with the cautery, but according to how much the third part of the eyelid is adhere.
Chapter 16.
On the cautery for the eyelid of the hole when the hairs are converted to the inside parts and poke the hole.
When the hairs of the hole are converted inward so that they poke the hole, there are two ways to cauterize them: either by cautery or by burning medicine.
If you cauterize it with fire, command the patient to pull his hairs so that they are straightened out, and while they are prolonged and curly; and if he pokes that, then you strengthen the hole with a bandage so that it does not get worse until the hairs grow back.
Then when they grow back and become curly, then place the patient's head in your lap, and carefully mark the eyelid of the hole with a tent, using a mark that is according to the shape of a dead leaf; and let the beginning of the mark be near the hairs.
Then place a damp cloth in white vinegar or in mullein tea on the hole.
And then light the cautery, of which this is the form:
And then cauterize over the marked area, little by little, in small spots, until the surface of the skin appears like a myrrh leaf, all appearing properly.
And the sign of your work's health is that you see the eyelid of the hole already contracted and the hairs of it elevated from the puncture of the hole.
And then raise your hand and lay him down for three days, and then place a bandage on him so that the scar is healed. And then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
But if the hairs return for any reason and the eyelid is softened, then do the cautery on it again all over as you did at first.
But if the hairs are in the eyelid, then cauterize that so that it is elevated to its natural place and is curly, and the hairs do not poke the hole.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you command the patient to pull his hairs so that they are prolonged and curly, and then let the mark be made of linen in the shape of a myrrh leaf.
And then take gum mastic and live coals, one ounce. Chapter 17. Take that mixture and spread it on the cloth that was made in the shape of a myrrh leaf, and place it over the hole or both holes; then place the patient' head in your lap.
And hold his finger over the medicine and press it gently so that the patient feels the biting of the medicine, for the biting is in him as if with fire.
And when you perceive that the biting has ceased, then remove the medicine and wash the hole with water; then consider if you see the eyelid already elevated as it is washed after the constriction by fire or incision, for then it is well; and if it returns to the medicine over the place where the medicine did not stick, and do not darken until your operation is healed and the hole contracted.
And then place a bandage on him so that the scar of the constriction is eradicated, and then treat it with an ointment of diascordium or another until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, after your operation, that you guard against the falling of the medicine into the hole; and if the eyelid, after several days, becomes softened and for some reason the hairs poke the hole, then repeat your medicine on that place of the eyelid which is softened, and especially as you did at the beginning, and bandage it until it is healed.
And this is the shape of myrrh leaf.
And know that men' holes are diverse according to their size and smallness, and therefore according to that is your constriction made.
And do not become experienced in this way to him who has studied this art.
Chapter 17.
On the cauterization of a fistula that is in the tear duct of the hole.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not succeed, then know that you cauterize it as follows: command the patient to hold his head in your lap, and let some attendant hold his head so that it is not moved nor shaken by his head.
And then place a damp cloth in white vinegar or in mullein tea on the hole of him.
And then light the cautery, of which this is its form:
Which is hollow, as it is shaped like a reed or a pen, from one end there is made the cautery.
And if you see that it penetrates to the other end. And if you want it to come out as a tent.
But what is hollow is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw out from it pus and dry that. If it is not open, then open it and extract the hernia.
Then place the cautery, which should be well ignited, over him, and hold your hand a little away from the cautery near the start of the nose so that your hand does not get burned or the patient become disturbed, and then apply the cautery to the openings of the hernia and corrupt that.
And so if at the beginning of your cautery it enters into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave it like this for three days, and then place a poultice on that spot and surround it with a dry bandage until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it has not healed, then apply an acrid corrosive medicine to the bone so that it is exposed and scrape it according to what is appropriate in its chapter.
And there is another way of cauterizing a fistula which some at first said: enter the place of the fistula and open it up and then in that fissure pour in subtle cement of which its shape is made; And melt into that the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with good retention so that the patient is not burned because the molten lead does not reach the hernia of that; and cover what you place over the hernia of the patient with linen or water, for the lead clogs the place of the fistula and heals it in a marvelous way.
And if the fistula is healed with what we have said about the cautery and treatment, that is good; and if not, then it is necessary to administer snuff of the nose and reduce the fistula into the body of the nose according to what will come in its proper place.
Chapter .xviii.
Of cautery for the fissure of the anus.
Many times fissures occur in the lips, and mostly in the lips of children.

Chapter .xx.

Chapter .xxi.
Of cautery for toothache.
When toothache is by coldness, or in it is frostbitten or rotten, or does not respond to treatment with medicine, then cautery in that is according to two ways: either cautery with a cow's horn, or cautery with fire.
Cautery with a horn is that you take a cow's horn and melt it in the embers of fire, and then take it with tongs and wrap it around the end of the tooth, and then dip that into the melted horn, and place that over the painful tooth quickly so that it is frostbitten.
And then repeat that several times so that the virtue of the fire reaches the root of the tooth.
And if you wish, dip wool or cotton in the horn, and place that over the painful tooth, and place ignited iron over it so that the iron reaches the cavity of the tooth.
However, cautery with fire is that you wait for the iron or needle to be red-hot, and there should be some thickness in it and the acuity of the fire does not reach the mouth of the patient.
And then ignite the cautery the form of which will come, and place that over the tooth and hold your hand so that the cautery is frostbitten; and do this several times because the pain will return again or on that day or the next day.
And if through the pressure of that cautery the patient fills his mouth with good saliva and holds it for an hour and then spits it out, this is the form of the cautery.
And cauterize however you wish according to what is possible for you.
And this is the form of the needle:

If its end is small, let one side be pointed and the other not; and the spatula should be of two sides like a lion's claw so that it does not injure the ear.

And if the obstruction is in the membrane of the ear, then take a fine reed and heat it with fire for just a little while, and then put that into the middle of the ear.
And therefore if you have sensed inflammation with what the patient feels with ease due to the difficulty of breathing, relieve the oppression of it with ease; and take care that you do not injure the nerves of the ear.
And then place over the ear a bandage of linen according to the size of the opening which you have wrapped around the egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure that the wound is healed; or else take some water and dip it in vinegar and sprinkle it on it, and if you see that the ear is already swollen, then take that off and replace it with another bandage which you have wrapped around cerated oil, until the swelling is reduced; and then treat it until it is cured.
But if blood flows from it, then soak sponge or bandage in warm water and place it over the ear, and use the other treatment which constricts the flow of blood, which is described in the treatise on distillation.
Chapter 8
On the cure for athelis which occurs in the eyelids.
Athelis which appear in the eyelids of some people are wet some days and dry on others.
Therefore let you have those with thong-like instruments or pincers, and split them with a spatula at their roots, and then place over the places thus split with oil.
But if pain comes with a flow of blood, then cauterize those with a small subtle lance.
And the better cautery is when the splits and wounds return as soon as any cause from their roots is removed; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burnt up and do not return.
Chapter 9
On the cure for granulation which appears in the eyelids of people.
Many times it occurs in the eyelids of people some cause which is likened to granulation in its strength and hardness, and because of that granulation is called.
And it is an accumulation of thick moisture in the eyelid above and below.
And its appearance in it is that you should consider if the granulation proceeds from something which appears in the eyelid and is swollen on all sides; and the cause of it is diverse.
Therefore open that with a slit according to its width, and then scrape it from all sides with ease until it discharges, and then stop that up with pincers and cut off those roots.
And if the wound is large, then you should stitch it up; and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not do harm to it unless its roots are restored and the flesh grows over it.
And if the granulation is declining at the edges of the eyelid, then cut away its surface and stop it up with pincers; and if you make an incision there, that will not be noticed in any cause.
And then the incision, wash the wound with salted water; and heal the wound with oil until it is healed.
Chapter X.
Of the cure of a ulcer in the eyelid above the eye.
Ulcers are grey in colour and are usually found on the eyelids above, and mainly affect infants. They harm the eyes of those affected, and they cannot bear the brightness of the sun because of this; therefore tears flow from their eyes.
And because of this you will see these people always looking down at the ground, and sleeping with their mouths open; and there is swelling and redness in their eyelids, and when you press on the eyelid, you can feel a lump underneath.
And a certain type of poultice is applied to the patient who rests his head on your lap; and you prepare a cloth and make it warm, and make a circle according to the size that fits the whole ulceration, and you place this over the ulcer and press with your fingers all around so that the pus collects under the poultice.
But in the primary stage, you must split the skin completely so that you reach the pus, for ulcers develop in this way through a fissure according to the shape of a white swelling.
Then extract this with cloths which you have wrapped between your fingers at the edges, and rub between the right and left hands until it is dispersed.
And be careful not to touch the ulcer with your fingers so that you do not hurt the patient while extracting it with the spatula.
But if pus does not appear at the beginning of the fissure, then touch it gently with a spatula so that pus comes out; and then extract it as we have said before; and then moisten cloths in water and vinegar, and place them over the ulcer and bind those with bandages.
And also apply salt to the wound and place it in the cavity of the fissure so that any remaining humidity is absorbed.
And then heal the ulcer until it is cured.
But if a scab forms on the wound, then heal it with soft poultices until it is healed.

Chapter XXIV.
On the healing of a wound in the nose.
When diverse meats are swollen in the nose, of which there is some cause that is connected to a scropion sting or to a polyps stone; and this ailment is called polypus because it resembles that stone represented. But if it is seething with blood or there occurs a boil, then you place on him that which you must; or if they are malignant boils, then you cut and cauterize them so that the blood is shed and all the holes are completely exposed.
And let there be spitting in the nose, with a mixture of vinegar and water or wine. But if the nose is open and from it proceeds humidity into the palate, then know that he is healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate according to how it should, then you know that within him is meat gone in the nostrils of which there has been no abscission.
And so then you must insert gall into him from which there is some thickness, and you see in him that there are pusy bones of the nostrils to which there has been no abscission; and let there be spitting in him of words with an accent on the first or last syllable, and insert between each nut the quantity of the finger or less, and put the patient to sleep and insert one end of the gall into his nostrils with a ray or another cause for which it is possible.

And then insert the two ends of the gall, one end which hangs over the nose and the other which is inserted in the nose, and administer a clamp of meat with the nostrils that are on the gall, and do this so that you know that the meats are at the nostrils of the gall.

Chapter XXV.
Of the eels in the ends of the nose.
Many times it is seen that there is a growth of an eel in the ends of the nose, and it swells and increases over several days so that it receives its guardant.
And for this you must insert it into his coming of his appearance, and remove all of that. And then place over the area a cautery or burning medicine that is in the location of him.
But if its abscission is delayed so that it swells, consider then if it is hard, dusky in color and of little feeling, do not touch that with iron because there is an ulcerous boil; for many times this happens to one who inserts that.
And if the boil is leonine and not dusky in color, and there is an abscission possible in all of it, then you must withdraw the patient and insert that without fear, and heal the area with something that comes out of it and constrict it so that he is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of the suturing of the nose, and of the lips, and of the ears when the continuity is dissolved by plague or similar.
Know that when there is a dissolution of continuity in one of these cartilages, there is little to be done in that operation except in some men.
Therefore, when for some cause or other, you find that it is a fresh plague with its blood, then you should bring together the two lips of the plague with a suture, and then cure that inside so that it may heal.
And if the lips of the dissolution of continuity are already separated, and each is made healthy, then you should cut off each part of its cord until that it bleeds. And then join the two lips with a suture and strengthen those, and over that pulverize dragon's blood and finely ground herbs, and place over the powder a piece of a diafinicon plaster or one of the other consolidative plasters, and leave it there for two or three days, and then remove that plaster and change the plaster and let it stay there so that the wound may heal by itself, and then cure with ointment until it is healthy.
But the manner of the suture is that you make the dissolution of continuity either with needles as we have recommended in the suture of the belly, or make the dissolution of continuity with a thread as we have said before and let us know.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those causes that come in the two lips on the inside of the parts.
Many times, in many men in the inside of their lips there are certain hard pustules that resemble err-grass, and some of them are smaller and some are larger.
Therefore you should turn the lips inside out and cut each of those hardnesses from all sides, and then close up the incision with finely ground sage so that the blood is constricted, and then wash it with wine vinegar and cure the area with something astringent until it is healthy.
Chapter 28.
Of incision of the flesh joined to the gums.
Frequently there is on the gum a piece of flesh which the ancients called albuginea, and so when you suspend that with an instrument or with your fingers and cut it away from its root, let the pus run out or the blood.
And then place over the area finely ground sage or other astringent or cauterizing powders.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it away again secondarily, and cauterize it, for the cautery will not return.

And so you should make a cut between your fingers and place the head of it in your mouth, and rub the teeth where those scales appear and the causes like sand until no cause remains from them, and that makes the blackness and the yellowing and the others go away so that they are cleansed.
And if that is what is at the beginning of the root, then it is good; and if not, then you should do the cutting over those again on the second or third day until it comes to the final state in whatever you want.

Chapter 30.
Of extraction of teeth.
You should cure the tooth of its pain with every trick and delay in completely extracting it, for there is no cause that exceeds the usual one when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance that causes an excuse in its extraction.
But when the tooth is forced to be extracted, you should certainly be certain which is the painful tooth, for many times the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and then you extract that one and the pain does not cease until the painful tooth is extracted, for we have seen this through the operation of the barbarians.
Therefore when you are certain which is the painful tooth and know it, then you should scarify in the circuit of the tooth with a spatulum on which there is some virtue so that it is dissolved from the tooth into the gum from all sides, and then draw it with tweezers or with subtle forceps bit by bit until you extract it.
And then press it strongly with great forceps for good speech or strong grip, and place the head of the patient between your thighs so that he does not move, and then draw the tooth according to rectitude in a way that you do not break it or give the patient greater pain.
And if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and place it below it from all sides with ease and force the extraction of it just as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is broken or rotten, then you should fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with the end of a fine wire so that it is not broken at the time of your constriction on it with forceps.
And you should open sufficiently with scarification in the circuit of all parts of the gum and guard your strength so that you do not break it and let the wound come apart from it, and return to the patient because of the malice so that his first pain is less.
And beware that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their haste and audacity about the extraction of them, for they do not administer what we have recommended; for many times they bring great illnesses on men which are difficult to extract, and their roots are all wrinkled, or some of them, or that you draw it with some bones from the jaw just as we have often witnessed.
And then the extraction of it should be washed in the mouth with wine vinegar or wine with salt.
And if it flows with blood afterwards, then grind some amount of alum and sprinkled over the wound, and if that alum does not work, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and tweezers with which the tooth is drawn at the beginning should be of long length, short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when you work with them.
And let them also be of thick handle within so that when you stretch them with those they are not bent, let them be short lengths.
And let them be made of iron of India drawn to a point with straight edges, and in the edges of those there are teeth of which some enter into others and stretch by strong constriction, and sometimes the edges of those are the shape of a goose beak which is hooked, it is bent, and they are of strong constriction.

Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of teeth that are prominent above the others.
When teeth are prominent not according to their natural form, and especially when this is the case for women, and especially for old women; then you must consider: if a tooth is next to another tooth, and it is not possible to cut it or file it, then pull that tooth out; but if it is not next to another tooth, then you insert that tooth with this tool whose form is as follows; it is like a pig's snout, and should be made of Indian iron with a pointed end.
And you must insert it in such a way that the tooth is inserted into it in two or three days because of the hardness of the tooth, and that no other teeth are affected.
And if it is a prominent tooth exposed to filing, then file that tooth with an Indian-iron file whose form is as follows:
And let it all be of Indian iron, and its edge sharp; and let it have fine points as with the file with which needles are made, and with which the tooth is filed little by little in several days with ease, so that no other teeth are affected or cut. Then smooth it out at its end, and cut away any blackness with a razor blade.
And if a tooth whose side is broken and which hurts the tongue, then you must file it so that the edge of that fracture is blunted and smoothed, and flattened so that the tongue is not hurt nor speech impaired.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of restoring broken teeth with gold or silver wire.
When teeth have received concussion and loosening through a blow or through falling, and the patient cannot chew on that side, and you have cured it with astringent medicines, and your cure has not been effective, then so that they do not loosen, there should be inserted in them gold wire and not silver wire, for silver will bend and after a few days will dislodge, but gold will remain firm.
And let the wire be of such thinness and thickness according to the quantity that can enter between the teeth; and the form of the binding is that you take the wire and pass it between two healthy teeth, and then let it grow with two curves of the wire between two broken ones, or if one or more, so that the thread comes to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then tighten the binding at the end from which you began, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not broken.
And let your binding be such that by which you bind the wire so that it does not come out.
Then insert the ends of the wire with forceps or tongs, and apply them in each place with tools, and fold them and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the broken one, so that they do not harm the tongue. Then let it be bound as tightly as possible.
But if it is loose or broken, then tighten it or those with another wire in the same way all the time.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the binding of two healthy teeth and two broken ones, bound thus as follows:

Chapter .xxxiv.
Of the cutting of the thread that comes in the tongue and prohibits speech.
This thread which appears under the tongue some times by nature or accidentally and occurs through a wound that has already healed.
And the operation in it is that you open the patient's mouth, and lay his jaw bone aside; and lift up his tongue, and then insert that nerve thread according to its width so that the tongue is loosened from its binding.
But if in it there is any hardness or knotting, and this exists because of a healing from a wound, then you cut it with a sharp instrument and split that according to its width so that you have cut the thread and the knotting is dissolved.
And beware lest there be made a crack in the bone flesh, and you split up here the hard part of the bone and blood flows out.
Then let it be washed with the dregs of wine or with vinegar, or with water in which there has been ground pepper; and then keep the tongue pressed down with a cloth, so that the patient does not speak again until it is healed.
But if there is bleeding, place powdered vitriol on the spot; but if blood comes out, then cauterize the spot with a lanolin cautery that is suitable for this purpose. And then treat that wound with the other remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 35.
On the extraction of a ranula under the tongue.
Sometimes an abscess forms under the tongue, resembling a small lump, which hinders the tongue from performing its natural functions, and it grows larger as it fills the mouth.
And the operation on it is to open the patient's mouth facing the sun and examine the abscess.
And if you see it is dark colored or black, hard, and the patient feels no pain when you touch it, then do not touch that spot because it is a ulcerous sore.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, and moist, then you should put the needle into it, and pierce it all around; and if blood comes out in the process of your operation, then you must place powdered vitriol on it to stop the bleeding.
And then return to your work until you have finished it, and wash the wound with sharp wine and salt; and treat that wound with other remedies for this purpose until it is healed.

And sometimes other abscesses occur in the ear canal besides those of the ears, and they are examined as mentioned in the examination of two spots equally.
And I have treated a woman with an abscess that occurred in her ear canal; the abscess was of a dark color and there was little pain, and it had closed her ear canal, and the woman could only breathe with difficulty, and she had prohibited herself from eating and drinking water, and she was nearing death if she did not drink water for one or two days.
And the abscess had swollen so much that it had formed two bumps for two months before they discharged.
So I treated her thoroughly and placed one of my needles in one of the bumps, and drew it out, and when I pulled it, a large portion came out; and then I examined that spot where I understood the other bump to be, and then I opened her mouth, pressed her tongue, and then I placed the needle in that abscess.
And then I cut out a large piece from it, and there was only a little blood. So that woman's ear canal was completely closed, and she began to drink water immediately, and then she ate food; therefore I did not cease piercing that abscess many times for a long time, and the abscess occurred in the same spot where it had been prolonged.
And then I made an incision and cauterized the vein of him, therefore until now; and then I walked around and did not know what God had done to me at that moment.

Chapter 40.
In which is the sum of the speech in the opening of sores and their cutting.
The sores are many species, of many manners, according to which I remember them, each one in this book.
And they are diverse in their piercing and cutting by two manners, of which one is by the species of sores in themselves; and by that we take from moisture.
And the second manner is according to the places in which they occur, for the sore that occurs on the head is different from that which occurs on the buttocks; and the sore that occurs on a fleshy place is different from that which occurs on a joint; and each one of them has its play by operation.
And there are some sores which only need to be成熟了sania in those or filling of them.
And there are other sores which need to be cut when they are crossed, and not mature according to their filling, such as sores that are near joints, for when a sore occurs near a joint, its nature is prolonged while it presses what is in the circuit of it, and strengthens the ligaments or nerves of that joint, so that there is a cause of contraction of that member; or it is near a principal member, and if you delay its piercing until it has become mature it injures the principal member; or it is near the buttocks, then pierce it before it matures; and do not cut it cruelly, because of incomplete digestion, but cut it near the buttocks so that it does not burst the part within the buttocks and is elongated to the parts inside the buttocks, and a fistula is formed, or it occurs in the end of it that it is not healed. But you should know the time of the piercing of the sore, and this is when it has matured.
And you should know that it has matured when there is a decrease in the pain and the cessation of fever, and also the decrease in heat and pulse, and the sharpness of the head of the sore, and the other signs.
And because you should know that the piercing happens in the place closest to the bottom of the sore and from pus coming from the healing of the eminence.
And the piercing should always be through the length of the flesh or body, if the sores are in the parts of the hands or feet, and in the places of the tendons of the nerves, and the strings; and at last, in all equal places where there is no reflection; for in those where it is reflected, use piercing according to that member.
But when the sore is on a fleshy place, then it is better that you pierce it before the digestion of it is confirmed according to its filling, as we have said; for if you pierce it before this, the part within it is prolonged and great dirt, and the lips of it are hardened and its depth.
But some sores are pierced according to the breadth of the flesh or body, depending on the necessity, or according to that by which the member suffers.
And you should administer a small piercing in small sores, and in large sores a great piercing and a wide one or many piercings according to the diversity of the size of the sores.
And there are some sores in which the vein is hollowed out and has been cut, when it has been done so that bread and made is in a term that it already is, as well as that which occurs in pimples and blisters.
And there are some that are cut with three angles; and there is one in which it is cut like the shape of a bay leaf of myrtle, as well as the sores of the angles.
And there are some in which round cutting and lunar cutting is administered, and they are like those.
But that one to which there is no head as are superficial sores, you should pierce that with superficial piercing, or flat only.
And when the sore is large and has collected a lot of sanies, pierce it and do not be afraid to extract all of its pus in that hour; take out some part from it, and then close the sore for another day, and then take out a larger part, and do this many times according to graduation until it is healed.
And especially if the patient is of a cold nature, or female, or child, or old in decrepitude, for the animal spirit is often resolved by the exit of sanies, and often the patient dies, so you operate them.
But keep this chapter with great caution, and then your piercing when you pierce these sores, cleanse the plagues and consider therefore if the plague of the sore is little, or it is a flat section, then you administer oil of lye or old wine; and if the sore is large and the fissures of the piercing are many, then you should stop wax in each hole so that it comes to points.
And if in the sore a part of the vein is cut or hollowed out around it, then you should complete it with old wine or linen thread made of linen, as well as when you have drawn out pans or roots, because of its moisture; and strengthen that within the third day; and then process it and heal it by what means, so that it comes to points.
But if bleeding occurs in the hour of your operation, then you should administer warm water and wine mixed with linen, and place that over the place of the bleeding many times.
But if the flow of blood continues, then you should administer remedies and treatments, and apply powders as I have recommended in many places in this book and in its divisions.
But if it is in the winter time, and the location of the ulcer is in a place with many nerves, then you should mix powders with wine and hot oil, and apply those to the areas.
And if it is in the summer, and the locations are fleshy, then you should apply powders mixed with water and oil or wine and oil, and keep that applied for three days as we have said, and then you should open the ulcer and clean it and administer to it what is suitable for its healing.
And this is what is necessary to know about the cure for ulcers generally, but according to the method of distinction I have already said something about every type of ulcer in a particular way.
Chapter 41.
On the section on ulcers that come in the skin of the head.
In the skin of the head there are small ulcers that contain small nodes made of tissues that belong to them ; and their tissues are made of many things.

Then you should repeat the application of Egyptian ointment on that wound second and third time if necessary, so long as you are sure that there is no cause of that rotted skin remaining  then treat the wound with ointment until it is healed.

But if an artery causes you trouble, then do what I told you before.
And similarly do in the case of a lapsified ulcer with section and treatment according to what we have said  and a section on a lapsified ulcer is less painful, for it has little blood and little moisture.
And sometimes some of these ulcers in the head are found in some people in which there is not moisture  and the cause of that is when someone hits an ulcer on the head of an old woman and find the ulcer like hard, rough, white stone which could not be pierced, and if someone hits it, it bleeds.
But in other accidental ulcers in the head, this is not the case, as in the ulcers that come to the heads of children due to teething, and the cause of them are the teeth  but the section of all these should be a superficial section.

Chapter 42.
On the section of scrofulas that occur in the neck.
Many times these ulcers occur in the neck and armpits and angles  and sometimes they appear in other parts of the body.

And it is more careful that a vein or nerve not be cut.

Chapter 43.
Of the incision of the lump of the apothema accidentally within the throat.
The ancients described this incision in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has performed it.
And this is the inscription of the speech of those men:
But in men who have squint, whoever performs the extraction of the epiglottis, by which all the veins and the main ones of the patient are here, should be avoided.
But in those who have apothemas in the mouth or throat or two ears, when the malady is not in the windpipe, one should use the incision of the epiglottis to avoid the loss that is caused by puncturing.
Therefore when we want to do this, we should cut the epiglottis below three circles of the circles of the epiglottis or four with a small incision for the width in that one which is between two circles for quantity, and the incision should be in the pharynx not in the cartilage, and that place is suitable for incision because it is deprived of flesh and its blood vessels are light.
And if the doctor is fearful, then he should extend the throat with an instrument and cut the membrane until he reaches the windpipe, but avoid the blood vessels if for some reason he sees any of them.
Then cut the membrane according to what we have recommended; and it is signified about the incision of the windpipe by the flame which goes out from that which is split in two, and also by the breathlessness.
And let the wound remain open for some time.
Therefore when the time has passed in which piercing is feared, bring together two lips of the wound's membrane and sew that alone to the cartilage.
Then administer medicines which cause the flesh within to grow so that it may heal.
And Albucasis says that the sum of the speech which we have recommended is that some of those apothemas have oppressed the throat of the patient and the patient has died within, and his breath has become weak, and some want to cut the epiglottis so that the patient may breathe over the wound by some respiration, and be saved from death.
And for that he commands to leave the wound open until the wickedness of the malady is consumed; and its wickedness lasts three days; and then he commands to sew up the wound and heal that which is within.
And that which I have seen by my servants is that a servant placed a stick and touched it in his throat and cut with it a part of the windpipe of the lungs, and he was called to heal him, and he found the breath just as it is breathed by the dead, and he discovered the wound and found the blood which had flowed from the wound small.
Then he confessed that he had not cut any vein or nerve and the breath came out from the wound and he healed that which was within so that it may be healed by fire.
And he did not listen to the servant unless he vomited in his mouth, and after some days he returned to the healing of his own dispositions.
Now he says that in the incision of the epiglottis there is no fear.

Chapter 45.
Of the incision of the species of nodes.
There are many species of nodes and I have already said all of their species in the division.
And let him who tells you the difference between a node and an exitura with doubt.
Therefore I say that with exitura there is heat and fever and violent pains until the ebullition of the superfluity ceases; and putrefaction is completed when the fever, pain, and heat cease.
But with a node there is no heat or fever or pain, and count that phlegmonic lion which is in the own bag.
And it is according to the color of the body, and its beginning is with a ulcer and it occurs thus with a pustule, whether major or minor, and they are according to two species for one is grassy and contains humidity, and the colors of humidity are many, as I described in the division.
Therefore when you come to the healing of a node, probe that and examine that at its beginning with an instrument which is called intromes according to what its form comes in the chapter which is nearby.
And it is the manner of inquiry of apothemas and of all nodes, and it is that you take this instrument and insert that into the most humid place which you find in the apothema, and you turn your fingers with that little by little until you know that the instrument has penetrated the membrane.
Then close your hand according to the measure of the size of the apothema, and extract the intromes and consider that which is flow out in its trail.
And if the humid matter flows out, of whatever color it is, then you cut that superficially just as I said in the other apothemas.
And if from the trail of the intromes you do not get out humidity, then know that it is grassy.
And dry over it with incision having the form of crosses according to this form: just as you taught him, and suspend that with hooks, and scrape the membrane from all parts with ease, and guard the skin or bag over that if you can, and extract that pus from the node.
But if it is torn in the operation, it is not possible to extract that pus and many times there, then you extract it at intervals until from it nothing leaks out for any cause.
Because if from it no leak out anything for any cause, little or much, the node returns according to the may.
And if you see that from it leaks out a little part, then complete the wound with some strong corrosive powders and close the wound, and pause over that which apothema cauterized, and heal that by another cure until it is healed.
And if it is a large node then you cut the two lips of it and heal that which is flesh.
But if the vein is contrary to it, or not pulsating, then you close and cover the place of zegi trissat which is vetriol and let that lie there for two days or three until its pus becomes thin and the boil of the blood; then return to cut that which is swollen of the node.

Chapter 49.
Of the incision of abscesses that occur through an artery or vein.
And the signs by which inflammation and abscess are known to be through an artery or vein: for when the abscess is through an artery, it is long drawn out in the body; and when you press on the abscess with your fingers, you feel a pulsing in it.
And that which is through a vein is round at its appearance in the body.
And an incision on this abscess is dangerous, especially in that which is below the armpits, and in the elbows, and in the neck, and in many places of the body; and it is very great, therefore its cure by cauterization should be avoided, and also that which is in the extremities or the head.
But on that which is above due to inflammation of the opening of the artery, make an incision in the vein along its length; and then open up the incision with tweezers, and free the artery from the membranes so that it may be exposed.
And then put a needle into it and make it penetrate to the other side, and constrict the artery with a double thread in two places, — which will cause you to see that in extraction of two arteries which are in the temples.
And then plug up the place between two membranes with spongy material so that the blood will all flow out of it and the abscess be dissolved.
And then administer a cure in which pus is engendered until the membranes fall away, and then cure it with suitable ointments until it heals.
And if the abscess is through an incision of a vein, then you should hold that which is by your hand to the abscess with the vein, and then thrust in the needle below the place which you have held by your hand, and make it penetrate; and have in it double thread so that you may extract it from the other side, and then tie off the abscess with good membrane according as I have said to you about the ligature of two parts with two extremities of the thread.
But if you fear that you will pull out the thread, then put in the thread with that needle below all of the abscess down to the incision of the first needle, and constrict your threads in four places.
Then dry up the abscess in its middle so that when it has come out of it, then you pierce the superfluity of the vein and leave that which is tied to it.
And then put on it powder which you have soaked in wine and oil, and then administer the cure which is made with cauteries and ointments until it heals.
Chapter 50 of the abscess which occurs from a twisting of nerves.

Chapter 51.

Chapter 52.
Of the cure of preeminence of the navel.
Preeminence of the navel is caused by many things; or by fissures in the septum which is over the belly, and there are from it the membrane or hernia according to what comes before in other ruptures; or by blood which flows out of a vein or artery as was said before, or by constricted distention within it.
But if it is by a fissure of the septum and by splitting of the membrane, then the color of the abscess is like that of the body; and there are in it pains, and then appears in various positions. And if it is by extrusion of the hernia, then its positions are like that which we have recounted, in more violent diversity, and when you press it with your fingers it is concealed, and then returns; and it is strong within it, and is great due to the entry of foments and vehement work.
But if due to humidity it is slippery and not hidden when you touch it with your hand, nor is it constricted or stuck.
And if it is due to blood, then these signs will appear as the ulcer turns black.
But if it is due to flesh, then the ulcer is heavy, large and hard, and it feels like a certain quantity.
But if it is due to windiness, then the touch of it is slippery.
And the operation on it is that you consider whether the protruding navel is due to an arterial or venous wound, or due to windiness; then you must abstain from treating it, for in that place there is fear and danger as I have taught you in the chapter where I said about ulcers from arteries and veins.
And if the navel's protrusion is due to the intestine or hernia, then you must command the patient to hold his breath and be standing upright; and then sign with your finger around the entire navel, and then command him to breathe in while you hold your hand over his chest, and then gently push the hernia back in with a strong ligature or a bandage, and the way of binding decently.
And then open the middle of the extended ulcer over the hernia and put your finger into that opening and seek out the intestine; and if you have caught it or held it by the ligament, then pull it out gently and push the intestine back inside the belly.
And if you've caught the hernia, then take that by the inch; and cut off the excess of it.
But if it is against your artery or vein, then surround that spot, and then return to your operation; and take two needles and put on them strong threads and insert the two needles into the circuit which you have made around the ulcer in the way of a cross, making the needles penetrate; and then tighten the ulcer in four places over the needles.
And if you want, take out the needles and let the extended flesh fall back and heal by itself; or insert into it what is subtle and healing, and then treat that spot with what is suitable for the wound to heal.
And if the navel's protrusion is due to flesh in it or humidity, then you must hollow out the ulcer as we have said and extract the blood or humidity which you find in it, and then treat that spot with what is suitable for it to be consolidated.
Chapter 53.
Of the cure of hernia.
We have already said, in the division, of the kind of hernia and in what manner there is a way to its cure by medicines, and that it is cured in it by iron without being ulcerated; and we have said that hernia is engendered in the mother and cautery of its cure.
And the ancients said that sometimes hernia is in a place where its removal is possible, like hernia in the breasts or in the groin and to those similar members allocated to be extracted entirely, especially when it is started small.
But when it is old and large, you must not approach it, for I have not seen that ulcer healed nor have I seen anyone else do so.
And the operation on it is when it is allocated as we have said, is that you precede and let the patient fast and purge black bile sometimes, and then cut that spot if a manifest fullness is found.
And then prepare the patient with a preparation in which he is located for operation, and then insert nails into the wound which are suited to that spot; and then hollow out every part with a good sharp saw without breaking any cause of the roots of it. Chapter 54.
Of the cure of hydrocephalus.
We have already recommended, in the division, of the kinds of hydrocephalus from water and the signs of each kind, of its cure by medicines.

But if you see the patient suffering from vertigo profuse, and in him there is no other malady but hydrocephalus only, and neither child nor old person, then the manner of its operation is that you raise the patient between your hands, and then press his belly with your hand, filling the water to the part below the navel, and then take a pointed spatula according to this form:
It is of two pointed parts long, of acute end as spatula; but indeed it has in it some brevity little by little, so that in the operation with that you do not pass into the intestine, and that you lead that.

But if the generation of hydrocephalus is from malady of the liver, then your section is left of the navel for three fingers.
And if its generation is from the bile, then your section is on the right.
And beware that your section is over the side over which the patient wants to lie so that the excess does not flow to that spot.
And then pierce with the instrument all the way through, and insert into that section, and raise your hand with the spatula between the navel and the hypochondrium, and you express that; and if it is the quantity of a fingernail or finger width.
And then pierce the hypochondrium so that the spatula reaches the place it wants to go, and it is the location of the water. And then extract that and insert an instrument of which this is the form: And it is similar to a subtle silver cannula; it is made of silver or brass or lead wire, and at the lower end of it there are two small holes: one from one side and one from the other side; and its end is split according to the manner of a feather, like this:
And at the upper end of it there is a ring, for when the instrument reaches the water, then that immediately slides over the instrument, therefore emptying from the water in the hour small quantity, for if you empty from that more than that does not befell in the hour, strongly harm the patient by resolution of his animal spirit, or accideth to him death which is approaching.
Now evacuate him according to the amount of his strength and according to what you know about the disposition of his sickness, the strength of his pulse, and his overall health.
Then remove the instrument and retain the water which causes it; as soon as it is retained by the cord, which holds the wound over the head of which you are told to pierce, according to that manner.
And then repeat the instrument on another day if the patient can tolerate it, and extract a small amount of water, and do this for several days out of caution, and beware of error until the water is hardly flowing at all.
But if you are concerned about the patient and leave out a great deal of water, then treat that wound with quicklime in sand, move it in a bath, and expose it to the sun for a long time; and treat that with drying medicines until it is healed.
And cauterize the stomach and the meld, and the vein from which the water is extracted, according to what I have recounted in that which precedes.

Chapter 69:
Of castration.
Know that castration in our law is forbidden, but it was allowed to me that I remember it in this book; and the reminder of it is not necessary except for two ways, of which one is for the doctor when he is questioned about it, so that he knows how to cure it if it happens; and the other way is that we many times have needed castration of other animals for our young by them, as with horses, and oxen, and pigs, and similar animals.
And therefore I say that castration is made in two ways: either by traction, or by incision into an incision.

Form for an instrument by which a woman is fumigated for retention of menstruation and the second month:
