And the narrow end should be placed in the vagina and the wider end over the fire, and the fumigation should be placed over the embers of the fire.
And she who has that within her should consume that fumigation and repeat another.
For treatment of the child's anus, if not perforated:
Young children whose anuses are not perforated, their rectums are narrow and closed. Thus the midwife who perforates that sphincter with her finger; and if not, perforate it with a sharp spatula, and beware that she does not touch the glans.
And then place wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and cure that with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is closed, then place a cannula in the anus for several days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And sometimes the closing of the anus occurs due to consolidation of a hematoma or of an abscess; thus that consolidation should be incised, and then it should be cured as we have said about the cure and the plug.
For treatment of the fistulas which appear afterwards:
The fistulas which appear afterwards near the anus discharge pus and cause a knob and swelling, and they appear close to the anus or in the space of one finger.
And it is a fistula or several.
And when that knob becomes old and discharges thin white or yellow pus, and sometimes some of these fistulas penetrate in several ways.
And those which penetrate are known through what exits from them by the orifice and by flatulence; and sometimes it uses the patient for an orifice of exit, and often it is expelled from them with worms.
And sometimes from those fistulas some penetrate into the space of the bladder, and into the muscle of the penis; and sometimes they penetrate into the joint of the hip and into the socket of the shoulder.
And that in which a penetrating fistula is known to have penetrated into the anus and not to have penetrated, it is because you put your finger inside the anus, and push a thin probe of horn or iron into the fistula when there is no pain.
But if in that there is pain, then push into it a thin probe of lead or hair from a horse's mane until you feel the probe or the hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel that at all and there is no process of excretion from the orifice, nor worms, nor flatulence, as we have said, then understand that it is not penetrating, and then turn to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or into the muscle of the penis, then its sign is the excretion of urine from that and prohibition of it so that the place is not consolidated with medicine.
But if it is penetrating into the joint of the hip or into the socket of the shoulder, then its sign is that a probe goes through there if in it there is no pain within the socket, and pain in the circuit of the part; and that from it there is discharge of pus at all times; and that it does not respond to medicine, nor is healed with ointment.
And in all those penetrating fistulas there is no cure of all, and the cure for those is labor and futility of the folk doctors about which they are established.
But in that which is not penetrating and does not become old, healing with iron is expected according to what you have recommended, and that which has already been done and proven.
And it is that the patient lies between your knees over his chest and lifts his legs up over you, with his hips tilted towards his belly, and then push a probe of lead or horn into the fistula if there is pain in it; and understand the place where the probe goes through.
And if that feels around the neck, then you put your finger inside the neck.
And if you feel in your finger the probe already placed discovered to penetrate, or the skin, then understand certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not labor over that, for there is no cure for that, as we have said.
But indeed some say that it is healed in some people with vinegar.
And of the medicine by which it is hoped to be healed immediately in him is that you ignite a small cautery according to the size of the fistula, as it has been preceded, and put that ignited into the fistula until it comes around the neck; and then repeat that two or three times until you understand that all those tightened fistulas which seem like a feather of a bird's quill are tightened.
And then cure that with waters wrapped in wool until all those tightened meats are healed, and then cure that with medicines which generate meat and consolidate it, and if it is healed, well; and if not, then it will not be healed at any time by another cure.
And if the probe goes in and does not penetrate into your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the probe which is evidently of meat or of sphincter, and the fistula on what it severs the surface of the membrane, then dry up the membrane from the beginning of the fistula, and you will dry them up with the probe, and it will be in the fistula until the section comes to the place in which the end of the probe comes out, and by the probe it is freed and cut.
And then cleanse those tightened meats which are like a quill of a bird' s feather and do not break from any cause.
But if flow of blood sometimes comes between you and your instrument when you push those meats, then the milk that is and the pus the leaner instrument is cauterized with fire or with sharp medicine, for cautery with fire tightens good two habits: it is tightening of those tightened meats, and abstention of blood by drying up of the moistness.
And then cure that with waters wrapped in wool or in cress until the place gets cold, and then cure the wound with hard meats which generate and consolidate.
And thus medicines in which there is stiffness and abstention; when then it is restored, it is already healed and do not fear recurrence.
But if the fistula has already penetrated into the anus and is elongated from the surface of the body, then insert your little finger into the anus and explore it with that.
And therefore if you feel with your finger and find membrane or flesh, and it is near the penetration, then there is no operation on it but rather by way of trust and hope.
That cause is treated in one of three ways: either cauterizing it as we have said; or cutting into it until you reach its cavity so that you can insert instruments and make the wound clean; nor should you cut at all so that you do not pierce the sac containing the pus, but instead make the patient void his bowels against his will so that he expels the pus from the anus. Then treat the fistula with what we have said as quickly as possible; or elongate the membrane and open the pustule around the anus, either by probing with a tent or another instrument with a sharp end, then clean those fleshly fistulas which you can reach by cutting into them; and try to make a complete cure of the whole wound by cutting, and if the upper opening is not healed and the fistula remains open in the anal region, it will discharge pus more quickly through the anus. And sometimes the fistula is finished in this way: it occurs when you insert the tent into the fistula, and there is pain around the anus on the surface of the body with the cord, and at the edge of the anus; then take a tent with a perforated end, which is made like the needle for piercing ears as follows:
and insert it into that fistula, wrapped in a piece of cloth or around the edges; and then insert the tent with the needle into the fistula until you reach its cavity.
But if it penetrates near the edge of the anus inside, then you withdraw the needle from that wound as you would insert your little finger into the anus; and pull out the end of the needle and bring the two ends together, and tighten each one, and leave them like that for a day or two, and when you have pushed the needle deeper into the flesh, tighten it so that those fleshly parts between the two ends of the needle are drawn in.
Then treat the wound until it is healed and consolidated.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way you are prepared to do, except into the rectum, for you should not pierce the sac; and then treat that until it is healed.
The shape of the splitting spatulas with which the fistula is split: one end is very sharp and the other blunt, so that it does not pierce what it should not, as I have said before.
Of suturing hemorrhoids in which there is blood in the incision of those.
There are two types of hemorrhoids: either they are between the anus like charcoal, or they are like grass fat. And there are small and large ones, and the blood flows from them at times; they may be internal or external, and on the edges of the anus.
However, those which are external to the anus are according to the months of little moisture; from which flows a watery fluid or a little blood with a continuous body; they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment for those which are inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and to squeeze so that the anus protrudes and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you grab them and pull them out with your nails or whatever you have them with your fingernail, and then cut those to their roots.
But if the nails are not gripped by them due to their moisture and softness, then take those with a rough cloth or a cord or a piece of wool, and pull them out with your fingers, and then cut them and probe their roots; or cauterize them as is preceded in the chapter on cauterizing. And then treat those with the other cure until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey to defecation, then anoint the patient with ointment in which there is a small amount of mordicant, and let him be washed with that water which is in the anus and let him squeeze out the excrement quickly when he has been anointed.
But if the hemorrhoids are external to the anus, then the cause of them is lightness; it is that you grab them with your fingernail or take them with a nail and probe them, and then treat them with what we have said until they are healed.
And he who has seen the incision, take a needle wrapped in cloth and insert it into the hemorrhoid and then probe with the needle into the root of the other part, and wrap both ends of the thread in the lower part of the needle, and tighten the hemorrhoid strongly with the thread; and cut the thread and pull out the needle.
And do this to all of them, and leave one and not cut it, so that the superfluous blood runs from that one.
And then place over the anus a cloth soaked in rose oil or cerot; and place that over the anus and command the patient to rest. And then let him void his bowels so that it comes out; and when it is defecated, treat those with ointment or another cure until they are healed.
And if they are old and not helped by that medicine, then take a tent with a sharp end or your fingernail and split them so that they become moist and let the edge of those above be split, which prevents those from healing; and then treat those until they are healed.
Then you dry them with a very violent friction so that there is bleeding and they are abraded; and then treat those until they are healed.
Know that hemorrhoids are a key; they are not unless the body is round according to the color of the body resembling the head of a nail, and it occurs throughout the body and especially in the lower parts: the feet and the fingers, and from these parts there is pain.
And then let that which is in the circle of the nail be cut, and let it be pulled out by its roots with forceps or nails, and then heal it.
And if you want, cauterize it as is preceded in its chapter.
But a wart is a small round asperity on the surface of the body, and many times it comes to children's hands.
And its treatment is that you stretch and probe it, or that you tighten it with a thread or nail so that it comes out. And if you want, cauterize it, as we have said, by fire or sharp medicine.
But a mole is a large asperity on the surface of the body near the anus; and when it is rubbed that mole is like a bite of an ant, and it occurs all over the body.
And the cure for it is that you take a penile catheter made of goose feather or vulture feather, which is strong, and place it over the fissure within which the foreskin contains the glans; then rotate your hand with the catheter so as to insert what is inside the foreskin and pause it in the flesh of the penis, thereby removing the foreskin from its root.
And if you wish, do this with an ergot or a needle according to this form:
Let the part above the catheter be drawn out with subtlety, silent (so that it is not perforated), wrapped up so that over the said part the sensation of touch and the revolution of it are abolished.
And if you wish to cauterize the foreskin according to what is said in the cauterization of atheyl in the chapter on cauteries.
Of the forms of instruments that are administered to give enemas.
Sometimes enemas are given with silver or lead, or melted ergot, or malleated; and sometimes they are given with those instruments small and large according to which they are administered, for that which is administered in the cure of little children must be small; and that of which the anuses are fissured or painful, the enemas of these should be small.
The form of a large enema and few:
The head of the catheter above, in which the pouch is tied, should be ample according to this manner; and it is to him distinction where the pouch is tied thus with threads.
And the extremity of it below, which is inserted in the anus, should be silent (so that it is not open) very much, drawing to subtlety; and on one side of it are two slits, and on the other side a slit, and the breadth of the slit according to the thickness of the rays and more gross a little.
And the pouch in which are placed the medicines of animal bladder or wool, and its root is a palm.
And it should be perforated with many slits thus as it is turned over; and if the space which is between the slits, the thickness of the said.
And then insert into those slits strong, raw bile which is of twelve biles, or pour it around them; and attach with that the pouch; and when it is placed on him the pouch, it should be bound at the head of the enema between two distinctions with a strong constriction, and then enema with it every slit: But the quality of enemaing with that is that you fill the pouch with medicine, which is warmed according to the quantity of the tolerance of the patient; and beware that it is not too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient should recline on his back, and elevate his legs up and place below him a bed or a bench; and then anoint the extremity of the cautery with oil or with white of egg or with mucilage of fenugreek or of similar; and then insert that into the anus with ease, and then the minister takes the pouch and squeezes it with two hands and empties with force into the enema all that is in the enema; and then pull out the enema and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces that to exit, let him hold as much as he can; and if he sleeps with that for a whole night, it is better for his healing.
But if you enema him in the illnesses of a swollen belly and his exit coats it, then repeat the cautery until he is healed.
And if in the anus are hemorrhoids or painful tumors, then let the enema be very subtle and silent, of those enemas with which children are enemaed.
Of the cure of sores.
You have already said in the division of the cure of sores what was convenient to the liver, and I say thus the cure of those with hand and medicines according to the complement.
Therefore I say that sores are diverse according to what causes sores and according to the place on which they fall.
And the causes by which many times sores are made are thus with stone, or incision of a knife or of awl, or impact with a spear, or with staff, or with arrow, or similar to those of such causes.
But the sores that are in the places of the body are thus with a sore which falls on the head, or neck, or feet, or belly, or throat, and similar to those of the members.
And I am counting the cure of some sores so that you pause that reasoning and rule over the other sores.
And I will begin of the sores of the head simple, for the remembrance of the compounded will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when it happens in the head a simple sore and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is by impact of stone or similar, and the tendon has only been torn and it is a great sore and you fear about that patient having an abscess, then you cut it and flebotomize the cephalica immediately, and do not delay that; and extract from him blood according to the quantity of his strength if there is no prohibitive cause of phlebotomy, mostly if the blood which exits from the sore is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the contrary of the side of the sore; and beware of the patient having repletion and of wine and coarse foods.
But if from the minor cause any prohibition, then it is diminished in food and do not come close to flesh nor wine.
And pause over the sore if it happens an abscess on him, cut it and pour into it oil boiled by itself or wine in which is stirred.
But if it is certain of an abscess on him, then pause over the sore if it is recent with his blood and the air has not changed that powder which I will count next shortly small.
And if the air has already changed that, then pause over it some ointments until it is healed.
But if the sore is great or is by incision of knife and similar, and its lips are not pulled together by threads, then pull those together with sutures according to what I will count of their sutures.
But if the sore is already uncovered of bone and pen, and there is no retention of it except for a small sling, then insert that and then cure the sore with medicines and restore the flesh after in place of tendon, and command to the patient that he abstain from foods in which is their thickness: caps and formen cut and similar to them, until it is compounded.
And if you putrefaction in the tendon and it does not adhere to bone, then you insert all that rotted and throw that out, and then cure that.
But if in these sores is artery or vein from which blood might flow, and it is not stopped by medicines, then you inquire over the artery, and if you cut that not-incised, then you insert that with spath, or tie that; and if necessity compels you to do what we have said, then cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these sores are simple and small, then the cause of them is cooling; also that you powder over those this powder, for when the air changes that.
And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of fennel one part, dragon blood two parts, not-extinguished three parts; let all be well beaten and sifted, and of that be the sore filled, and let it be bound with good constriction so that it presses on that until it hardens, and let the blood coagulate.
And then tighten that above with powder and let it stay like that until the patient is healed and the medicine has taken effect.
And sometimes they do this alone when they are not in the presence of their senses and dragon's blood.
And they make this operation on great wounds when it is convenient that it be applied directly to the wound and tightly bound, and on fresh wounds with their blood, which the air has not altered.
And if the air has altered them, and especially in the summer, it is not necessary to apply this powder, but rather heal those wounds with what we have said before, by placing on them some cooked ointments or a poultice made of wheat flour mixed with water and honey until they are healed.
But if it happens that there is a broken bone in the wound, and it is small, then you should remove it with tweezers.
And I will describe how to heal fractures of the head when the skull has been damaged.
Of wounds to the neck:
But if the wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference in its treatment from a wound to the head when it is simple.
But if the nerve of the neck is cut, or an artery, then there should be no movement of that area but rather it should be treated with something that makes a strong contraction, like vinegar and vitriol, which are similar to one another in their effect on nerves and blood vessels. And you should apply nothing to the wound that is hot, for the substance of the nerve is very sensitive, and its connection goes through to the brain, which is the most important part of all the members.
And when it is healed by remedies that are light, like washing it with warm water, or applying some ointment made of rosewater or not, and then drying it in the sun until it is healed, as well as using moistened bandages.
But if the wound is large, then use stitches or close up the lips of it, and heal that area with powder until it is healed.
But if the wound has depth or there is suppuration in the wound to the lower part of it, then pierce it in the lowest spot.
But if an artery is cut and there is bleeding, then stop it or dry it up, or ligature it, or cautery it if necessary to do so.
But if the vertebrae of the neck have been split or broken, and the veins of the neck have not been cut, then you should close up the lips of the wound with stitches along the bones of the vertebrae and not touch it; before doing so, bring back that area to its natural shape, and then tightly bind it with a decent bandage, and keep it like that for several days.
But if the wound has been caused by pressure on the bone, then you should pierce it, and if there are any fragments of bone in the wound, extract them all; and whatever is not apparent, let it be exposed so that the wound can suppurate, and the pus can drain out.
And if the wound is fresh, then powder it with the powder that has been recommended to you. But if there is a fistula in the wound, then heal it with something that I will describe later in this chapter.
If it is a wound from a lance or javelin, and you see how deep it is, then know that such a wound is usually fatal.
And if there is no depth to it and the wound is fresh with blood on it, then you should apply powder to it in the first hour, and not tighten it so that the blood is not retained in its depth but rather returns to the heart and leaves the patient, but rather apply an attractive ointment, and wash it twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available, then you should stop up the hole of the wound with a piece of wool so that it sucks out what is moist inside; and make the patient sleep on the wound so that what is inside flows out.
But if three days or more have passed and the patient has no spasms nor trembling of the heart nor stricture of breath, and sees all other good signs of healing, then know that the wound is saved; and then heal it with poultices and with the other remedies until it is healed.
But if healing does not happen, and the wound has been open for a long time, then know that it is a fistula; heal it therefore according to what I have described in its chapter.
And if the wound is from a spear or javelin and is simple on the surface of the foot or back, then heal it with something that precedes stitches if it is large, or with powder if it is small.
But if there has been pressure on the bone, and only a little of it is split, then you should twist the wound and close to extract those pieces, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then let them be exposed so that the wound can suppurate, for their extraction is easier when it is suppurating.
And for other accidental wounds on the other members, the treatment in healing is the same as what has been said before.
Of wounds to the abdomen:
The disposition that occurs in the abdomen is either great, or small, or middling.
And the disruption is made difficult in another way, and it is known that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not close the insertion of them into their place at once, for they open up and are swollen so that the insertion of them is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is middling, for in that one the reduction of the intestines is not difficult, just as it is difficult in both species mentioned before.
And know that the suture of the abdomen is according to four methods, but two sutures are common and convenient in suturing the other wounds of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to the other wounds, but however they are proper to the suture of the abdomen.
And I will tell you about each one with explanation and description.
Therefore I say that when the wound is small and there is some cause of the intestines through it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then it is one of two ways: or because the disruption is small as we have said before, or when there is inflammation of the intestines because of coldness in the air.
And therefore when that is so, then know that you should warm it by soaking a sponge or cloth in lukewarm water alone, or gather in squinant water, and cyperum, and spic, and embrocations over it to resolve the inflammation.
And sometimes it does this in which there is stiffness, and it is better than plain water in resolving inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation resolved, then you should moisten the abdomen with water in which is steeped mallows and alder, for by that the insertion of it is eased by a little cause.
But if it is necessary to use an instrument for this cure, then cut the hemorrhoids with a small instrument that has been sharpened.
And the first cure is better when it is not cutting and there is no need to dry it out and weaken it.
As for the instrument, here is its form:
One end of it should be curved and sharp, the other not sharp, and the tip slender, though not as slender as a stylus, but rather blunt.
And so when the wound is enlarged and the intestine has come out, then its reduction should be according to its natural shape, in its proper place, if possible on top of it, for that is best.
But if the hemorrhoid is large and it is in the lower part of the abdomen, then have the patient lie on his back, with his legs higher than his head; but if it is in a higher part of the abdomen, then let him lie on his side, with his head and higher leg on top.
And similarly if the hemorrhoid is on one side of the abdomen, then pay attention and consider that the side with the wound should be higher than the other.
And so you should administer this for both large and small hemorrhoids.
And for small hemorrhoids, have ready what you have prepared, and let the minister apply it between his fingers, which should hold the hemorrhoid firmly and close its orifices, looking out for any cause of bleeding in both directions.
And this is the common way of suturing: take the needle and thread according to the amount of the wound's amplitude; and let it go from the tip of the hemorrhoid of the little finger, and tighten the thread, making sure you do not put oil on both ends of the thread, using two fingers that are under the thread to make the other end penetrate; and you have joined two ends of the thread and two ends of the needle, and so four knots are made.
And tighten with double thread around the needle, and several times from both sides, and join together the two lips and the hemorrhoid by decent stitching; and leave the thickness of the finger, and put another needle in it, and tie that with thread as it is bound with the first needle; and do not cease doing this if it is necessary with needles until you have completed all the stitches of the wound.
And let the needles be smaller between thickness and thinness, for thin needles pierce the flesh more easily; and thick ones are difficult to use in the thread, and so for that reason let them be smaller in thickness but not in grossness.
And let the needle not be put in both ends of the thread at once, lest the flesh be pierced too easily and the hemorrhoid bursts before it is healed.
And let the stitches not be prolonged so that the wound is not prohibited from healing.
And insert the ends of the needles so that they do not hurt the patient during his sleep; and spread powder on all sides over the stitches, which will hold the ends of the needles; and let them remain until you know that the wound is healed.
And this kind of stitching with needles in whatever way is more convenient for small wounds, for it has one needle or two or around them.
But the second stitching is common: and it is that you stitch the four ends, of the thread and two ends of the needle, in one go, with a needle in which the thread is wrapped, both thin and thick.
And when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, reduce the needle from that lower part from which you began, so that the thread drawn back draws the upper part of the wound to the wound, so that the stitching is like a stitch in cloth with which clothes are tightened.
And leave between each stitch the thickness of the little finger.
And this stitching is known to men.
And with such stitching I closed a wound which was inflicted on a man in his abdomen, and it was a wound from a knife, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and it had come out around two palms from the hernia of the middle part, and the rupture was in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had come out through the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was healed in the space of fifteen days or around that, and I cured him, and he lived for many years, and was restored to his senses.
And other physicians had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle I cured him with ointment, for I did not heal him with poultice nor any medicine, but I put a cotton pad over the wound twice a day and washed it often with water of honey until he was healed.
But I will tell you two kinds of stitching proper according to the narration of the Word.
And it is that you begin with the thread, and insert the needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And so when the needle penetrates in the thread and in that part from which you begin, straight and throughout all the body, let the end of the needle be in the part where you have inserted it; and make the other end penetrate from the inside part to the outside in the other end of the wound.
And when you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate another time in that end of the wound of the little finger from the outside to the inside; and let the end of the needle be in that side, and make the needle penetrate in the other end of the wound of the little finger from the inside to the outside.
And make it penetrate with that penetration with which you are making it penetrate in the needle in the end of the little finger, in the end of the needle which is in the wound until it penetrates through all of it.
And begin from that end and sew it with the end which is of the needle in the other side, and draw the needle from the thread which has penetrated, and then reduce it in that thread and sew the end of the little finger which is on the other side with that end of the needle; and draw the needle from the thread which is of the part of him.
And do that once and again, and do that once and another time until you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this way of stitching is that you sew with skin stitching, just as you sew the skin, so that you sew the hernia one time from the end of the thread, and let it remain until you have completed it.
But the second proper stitching which I teach Gualia, and it is of wound of man and by him recomposed, is that you sew it according to similarity, just as other physicians sew it, in that they join all parts to him conveniently by nature; and apply one end to another end of him, and that is done according to how I have recomposed.
Let the needle be in the end of the little finger at your side from the outside to the inside, and make it penetrate to the inside to the outside in two ends of the hernia; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in two ends of the wound.
Then return those bones to their original position, and ensure that some of the bones inside line up with the corresponding bones on the outside at both ends of the fractured area, specifically those on the opposite side.
This stitching method is better than common stitching, and it is the stitching for which I gave instructions earlier.
This is the speech of Gualia:
And some say that stomach stitching is done with two needles; you should insert one needle into each wound and then bring the needles together from opposite sides of the wound, just as you would stitch clothes.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the abdomen, then stitching it is more difficult than stitching other places in the abdomen.
But the treatment of plague is judged based on the other plagues.
The cause is that when you encounter a fresh plague with its blood showing through the skin, and you reduce the hernia and warm it up and dress it appropriately, then apply an embrocation powder to it.
And if the air has already affected the plague, then apply some unguent to it that you have at hand, one which hardens like stone and closes up the wounds; then dress it as you would any other plague until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines with you, then apply it immediately, for as soon as it begins to ooze pus and is changed from time to time during the day, two or three times a day, depending on your instruction, until it is healed; for in many cases simple plagues are cured without any other treatment.
But if you fear that the plague will spread to the main limbs, then soak lion wool in warm or lukewarm oil and apply it around the area between the ankles and below the knees.
And if he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, for as many times as that happens to him, he should anoint that with black styptic tea, especially if the putrefaction is caused by an intestinal hernia and there is a penetrating exit wound from it.
And know that the treatment for a hernia of the large intestine is easier than that of the small intestine.
And it is more difficult to heal because the intestine called the "duodenum" does not receive healing from any plague that occurs in it due to the great multitude of veins that are in it and due to its size compared to those, and due to the subtlety of its body and its proximity to nerve nature.

Of the cure of sores and the quality of enemas with medicines.

By removing arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their species, and according to the parts of the body where they are found, because their diversity is that some have great points, and small, and concaves, and blind, but others are hollow; and some have three angles, while others have tongues, and still others are like stars.

And to him who is full and strong and needs bloodletting at one time, the phlebotomy of the vein should be enlarged, and the phlebotomies should be wide.

And if the vein is inflamed, then leave that and do not touch it until the inflammation is settled.

Of ventilation and of the qualities of using those.
Ventozes are of horns, or of copper, or of glass.
And ventilation is done according to two manners: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventilation without scarification.
And this ventilation which is without scarification is according to two manners, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And to the ventozes which are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are fourteen locations of the body, of which one in which ventozes are paused is alnocrati, and it is the posterior part of the head; and the middle location of it is from two parts of the neck together; and ventozes of the chin below the mandible further down from the mouth; and ventozes of two spatulas; and ventozes of alohoster, and it is above the end of the beard; and ventozes of two little horns, and they are of two arms; and ventozes of two ankles; and ventozes of two thighs.
And ventilation does not extract blood except from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and by this it does not have the virtue through them, just as it is through phlebotomy; nor do we believe that ventilation should be done with fire or without fire in any disease made by fullness within which the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts for ventilation for disease or for continued, we administer that throughout the hour at the beginning of the month and in its end, and in the middle of it and whenever it is necessary.
And therefore when it comes to one of these causes, and especially if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we command ventilation be done after two or three hours have passed of the day.
And likewise of ventilation from the nape is that when it conferes to gravity in the head and to that which is bent down into the eyes; but if that is, then let there be proper evacuation of all the body.

And if not, then wash the spot with sweet wine and then with water; and it should be rubbed and expressed.

And if the blood is flowing profusely, then powder vitriol, or gall nuts and similar astringent substances over the wound to stop the flow of blood; or place cooked broad bean pods on the wound and let them adhere to it until the blood flow stops.
And if necessary, repeat the application of leeches as often as possible.

And when you want them to stop, then powder them with some ground aloe, or salt, or ash, and they will immediately.
Thus begins the treatment of the third part: restoration. This chapter is very important in the art of medicine; it is the restoration of fractures and accidental displacement in the bones.
Oh son, know that the foolish doctors and the common people and those who have not read the books of the Ancients nor understood a letter from them make boasts about this chapter.
And for this reason, the way of the wise in our land is hidden; and I have not been obliged to do that at any time; and I have not used what I learned, except through long study of the books of the Ancients and understanding their wisdom, so that I could extract their science.
And I have added to that through experience and study over the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science and experience tell me about it; since I have condensed it for you, freeing you from the obligation of reading; and I have condensed it at the end of the discussion; and I have demonstrated it at the end of the demonstration; and I have formed it into many forms of instruments which are administered in this way, as I have done in two preceding chapters.
Chapter First.
Summary and collection of the conditions for which a fracture of bones is necessary, from which before we begin to discuss the restoration of broken and various members of the body. Now, therefore, let us begin the introduction to this chapter with summaries of our speech; and the chapters which are understood by artisans at first sight and at a standstill over their truth, as you and those who are prompt to know or learn this noble art from you.
And therefore I say that when it happens to someone a fracture or displacement or twisting or hernia, then you should first treat them with phlebotomy or the application of that, or whatever poultice, if no contrary cause prohibits it, such as debility of strength, or is that person an infant, or old and feeble, or in the time of vehement heat, or of vehement rubbing.
And then let them eat food made of fresh herbs and of birds' meat and crab apples; and refrain from thick meats and filling foods that fill the veins with blood, so that they do not fear the appearance of pus in the body.
And then return the patient to their usual diet.
And therefore when the broken bone begins to be restored, then let the patient eat nourishing foods of food rich in nutrients, thick and heavy, such as foods made from wheat cooked with water, and rice, and peas, and beans, and the bellies of cows, and hogs, and fresh pigs, and gross wine, and similar to those, and on this diet coagulation of the fracture occurs more gently and better.
And know that the bones break more when they are in people who are in their prime and feeble, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of their members.
But nature adjusts and connects the broken bone of all parts for no apparent cause like glue in which there is thickness that sticks and joins together so that it is strong at the end, as fire at first, so that no contrary cause prohibits it from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary that the patient eat foods which are heavy and sticky and thick, as we have said.
And know that the kinds of fractures are diverse according to the kinds of members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the toe; and similarly all the other members are diverse among themselves.
And I have experienced all kinds of fractures, explained in my own chapter distinct from others.
Many kinds of fractures within themselves. And the kinds of fractures of bones in their joints are diverse; for there is made an arched fracture when it occurs in them in stars; and some times the fracture is according to the length of the bone: and so the fractures are of the sitis, and knuckles, and accessories, as well as those which occur in the hand, and a crack, and disruption in the head, and a fissure fracture.
And there is great pain or torment inherent in the restoration of each according to what comes to mind about it, distinct from the others in their locations.
A sign of bone fracture. And by this is known a fracture of the bone: its crookedness, and appearance of it to the senses, and feeling of it when you apply your hand to it.
And therefore if there is not a crookedness visible in the place, nor any feeling of it when you touch it with movement and the patient does not find great pain, then there is not here a fracture.
It is possible that there be crookedness or a small crack, then you should not groan when you touch it with your hand and with compression of all; but pause upon it medicines of which you have remembrance properly at the moment which suits the location; and let it be bound closely with a subtle bandage.
And know that the location when it is arched and not split into two or else anything that does not happen in it satagium, as all the parts of its comparison are removed, then it behooves you to commence from your hour to the rectification of it have that to it happens apostema caut.
And if to it happens apostema cautery, then let it be that for some days until the apostema ceases; and let it heal that second in whatever manner you can of elevation and ingen.
And know that restoration of it and piercing of it is less difficult than of the bone in which happens satagium.
And let it be bound closely according to what will come the reminder of it.
But if in the bone it is satagium, then extension of this broken member from two parts is necessary, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or one cord and the hand.
And let your position be such that you pause the member in its natural location according to which the parts are extended of the broken member, then you must pierce these accessories with ingen and according to whatever manner you can in their locations.
And guard you according to your strength that no pain or harm happens over the patient by your operation of pain or lesion; and assure you according to your ability that you apply one bone to the other in companionship according to their best forms.
And it behooves you in that hour profitably to touch with your hand that, and if you see here any diverse cause, rectify that and heal that by the quantity of your skill.
And avoid violent extension and compression, as also with foolish doctors who many times make happen apostema cautery by their operation, or make happen contraction in the member as we have seen this by their many operations.
And the quality of the stricture of the broken member is that which you count; for you know that the broken members are differentiated in their smallness and in their greatness and in the shape of them.
And those that are small as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to them, then let the bandage be of very subtle cloth.
But those that are thick as the thigh or the calf, let there be a hard bandage in them, for the tight bandage connects the large member and tightly that from all parts with even stricture, so that in it does not enter rottenness.

Another method of preparing a restorative plaster for fractures and dislocations and torques: take assafetida, opoponax, acacia, enula, and myrrh, which is the resin of wild mastic trees, all in equal drams; myrrh and aloes, each five drams; aloe juice or egg white if the patient's complexion is scorched; and use this plaster, for it is well-composed and quickly restores broken bones, and many men have benefited from it.
Another method of preparing a plaster that benefits fractures and torques: take mastic and white hellebore, each ten drams; myrrh and aloe, each five drams; acacia six drams; Armenian bole twenty drams.
