Method of plaster for breaking open and administering the restoration of broken bones, and also to resolve the residue of wounds: take powdered aloes, camomile and violet flowers, and flour of linseed, of all these an equal portion .i.; Let all be sifted and let them be mixed with wine, if the member is not wounded.
But if it is wounded, then cover it with water of celandine or vinegar, and use it.
Another method of a stronger plaster for resolution, when apostema happens due to the restoration of a member: take powdered aloes and licorice root, and poppy seeds, and balm, and marjoram, violet flowers, camomile, all these in equal parts i; let all be sifted and mixed with water of sage or warm water or wine, according to the quality of the member and the condition of its color.
But hypocras does not dry in the air, so it must be laid over the broken member to restore it; nor can it be made of wax and oil alone without another cause; and it should be applied between thickness and thinness.
But it is known that they are laid over the broken member to restore it, and its causes as well as if there is dryness with heat in it; for this reason also myrrh, incense and similar things are added according to those causes which we have mentioned.
Method of plaster that helps with injury and pain: take miel, and cedar wood shavings or pine tree bark, and aloes, and salt, all in equal parts; let them be sifted and mixed, and let a plaster be made from it.
But the amount according to the extent that the binding covers, and let it be bound tightly, consider if there is not pain nor bruise nor broken bone, and it does not hurt in its place; then do not loosen it for several days. And if there happens to the affected area vehement pain or bruise or swelling, then cut or loosen it immediately, and remove the plaster from it.
And take a piece of linen and marine sponge that is moistened, and let the affected area be washed with mild water; and with this wash the affected area until the pain ceases and the swelling is reduced; then let it rest for one hour; and let wool soaked in vinegar and oil or roasted oil be laid over it for one night, and be sure of cauterization, and let the swelling of the member be reduced, and the pain of it be eased.
And then tighten it gently and apply a small plaster lightly, and not tightly with your first tightening, and make it subtle by that until it is healed.
And if the apostema and heat and pain and swelling and all the accidents are already reduced to nothing, and you have removed plaster and tightening, then do this in the same way as before.
And if there does not happen to the member any cause of those which we have mentioned, then do not loosen it except after three or four or five or seven days; and some times it is left for 20 days according to how you see from the loosening of the member, thus as we have said, until the fracture adheres and comes close to the coagulation of the flesh over it.
And then tighten it gently but not as in your first tightening, and loosen it according to the increase of the swelling of the patient's body, according to how it is preceded by its recovery.
But if you see the site of the fracture already dried and weakened but not covered, then know that nourishment is prohibited from coming to it; and therefore moisten it with mild water due to total loosening that of the patient, and let this be for three days; and loosen the binding a little, and by this operation the member will recover more quickly.
But that which the fols of the restorers of the broken member do another loosening when it is not restored at the beginning, according to how it covers, and is restored according to crookedness, is error according to their operation, and great fear, and if they were drawn out by force in their free sites and were hit against it.
And I have not seen any of them doing that, so that it is most reasonable that it does not happen.
Chapter according to: Of fracture when it happens in the head.
The species of fractures are many, and the forms of them are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for any fracture is not made by the blow of a staff, and the blow hits all the bones inside the skull, which is under the skull, thus as it strikes in the trunk, and by this that type of fracture is called ascial; or the staff enters a part of the bone, and enters the flesh of it and does not pass the incision into the interior of it; and this type of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures is large or small, and from the fracture which is crushed or broken; and its cause is impact with a stone or fall on a stone, and those similar to it.
And this fracture is penetrating, approaching the membrane below the bone, or it is on the surface of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures is large or small.
And the fracture that is concealed in the bone, in the thickness of the hair, and because of this such a type of fracture is named capillary.
And fracture that is caused by impact or fall on a stone, or something similar, that forces a thin layer of bone to enter the interior, and this occurs in the concave location just as it does to the teeth when they suffer an impact and a part of their interior is broken off.
And most often this occurs in the bodies of elderly people, just as it does to children's heads.
And all these species of fractures are set apart like stars separated. And you must use a bandage for all this in its place.
And we know all these species of fractures by detection over them and by investigation with our fingers and observation of the corrupted flesh that is over them.
And capillary fracture is known by its being uncovered on the bone and washed clean with a sponge, for the fracture appears black.
But in the cure of fractures consider first the accidents of the patient, and then if you see from these accidents that something signifies by manifest indication regarding fear, such as vomiting of cholera, and extension, and loss of understanding, and loss of voice, and fainting, and acute fever, and discharge from orifices and redness of these, and similar to them in the major dispositions, one who is not healed.
And if you see accidents that do not run their course, then you expect health, then you begin the cure of him.
The reason for this is that if the wound has come to you in the beginning as it was wounded and this is in the days of winter, then consider that you study in the appearance of the bone before the day fourteen according to all disposition.
And if it is in the days of summer, then consider that you cook the bone before the seventh day, having that which is below the membrane be corrupted from the bone, and that accidents that we have counted occur.
And if the fracture has already arrived at the membrane covering the brain and it is in small pieces and crushed, then consider that you insert the crushed and ground-up part according to how I will tell you.
And it is that you wash the head of the wounded and uncover over the bone according to custom and manner possible to you according to the figure of the wound, and let it be lighter on the patient.
And if it happens to you that you must do the detection of the bone, flows of blood or pus cauterized, then you must oppose it with what is proper, and it is that you apply the location with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest while the apostema is sedated, and be certain of the flows of blood; and then begin on the circuit of the bone and in its ablation.
And this must be according to one of two manners of operation.
And the one manner is that you insert the bone with a narrow incision knife, and this is its form: And then administer another incision knife this one a little wider than that one; and this is its form:
And the other manner is that you excavate the bone with a wide scoop, and approach it by the sides according to manner possible to you. Then administer the incision knife wider than the first one.
And you must cover the bone according to manner possible to you, and cover with flesh that is over it and cover with a wide scoop, and cover by the sides. And if the fracture is crushed or broken, then you must cover the bone with a narrow incision knife, and cover with flesh that is over it, and cover by the sides.
And use them with facility in impact on the incision knife so that it does not concuss the head and lead that one.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then consider that you pierce in the circuit of it before you administer the incision knives with the drills which are named non-deepening drills.
And they are not named thus but because these do not pause the end of the bone until it is directly below it, for the drill has rounded extremity or edge over that which is below the head of it like an edge or small circle, and this prohibits from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take from these drills great number of which each one convenes the thickness of every bone, while being present in all skulls of the brain the extremity of which is acute, and the quantity in length and brevity is according to the quantity of thickness of this skull.
And if because of what is below the bone flows of blood or pus cauterized, then you must oppose it with what is proper and approach the head of the patient to it with a wide scoop according to manner possible to you.
And then insert with incision knives that which is between each traction of the bone, and do that most subtle thing that you can over that at end of elevation, as we have said, while erradicating the bone, or with your hand, or with any other cause by some instruments which you have equipped for it, as tweezers and forceps.
And consider that you guard with total caution so that the incision knife does not touch any cause of the brain.
And then when you have circuit the bone and it is separated from the brain if it is continued with it, draw that out, and polish and smooth that in the asperity of it of what is in the bone with another instrument similar to an incision knife but however be more thin and subtle than the other incision knives.
And if you draw out any small pieces of the bones and jagged, then take that with facility with what is proper for you from the instruments; and then cure the wound with ligatures and bandages which we have said.
And the other manner of operation is lighter and distant from fear, which Gualia says and it is called by great lawgiver; and this is its speech: It is necessary for you in the beginning that you begin to uncover the part of the bone of the location in which there is the fracture most vehement and most manifest, while when you have uncovered that part and you have paused below it the extremity of this incision knife slow and gentle, of which this is its form:
And it is that the lens is a lion and does not contain any cause, and the acute part is from him in two sides in front in length, as well as times; that the lens be adjusted to the head with the face of him incident acute in the bone.
And then you pierce the scissors of one part with a small mallet inside so that they cut all bones with ease, just as they are turned, and you be sure of the membrane that nothing happens to him accidentally, in any way, and if the medicine was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
If there is any cause attached to the bone of the membrane in any place of bones, cut that from him with the extremity of the scissors lenticular, and deliver that from him with ease, for he is delivered from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the membrane, and what is cut off or damaged from the bone is the face of it, and remains in him roughness and small thorns, then cover that roughness and remove those thorns with sharp razors by which you have prepared the number, of various quantities so that it may be possible for you to administer some of them in every place of the most convenient ones, and the most right ones according to what you are led astray by your operation of that broken bone and its form.
And cover with your reason with which you shave the bone at the beginning of the sharpest side of those razors, and then use it with the most subtle one of it, and do not cease to do this according to continuity until you come to administer the thickest and the most subtle of all those.
But in any other small capillary and subtle fractures, cover that they may be cured according to what you are led astray by the rectification of it.
And it is a cause that is not hidden from all those who have studied in that art. And we have written about what we have shown in great fractures.
And if the membrane is stretched when you shave the bone, then take cloth according to the quantity of the wound, and soak that in rose oil, and place that over the hole of the wound; and then take another cloth folded or doubled and soak it in wine and rose oil and place that over the first cloth and do this most gently about that so that you do not disturb the head; and then administer on that lye, and do not tighten it except according to the quantity which the wound has so much.
And leave that with this cure for a day or two until you are sure of cauterized pus; and then unbind it and administer medicines with which there is superfluous dryness, such as lint and linseed flour, and powder of onions and of aristology, and of similar things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder which is sprinkled on the wound as they are dried; and generally, administer in that all medicine of the property of which there is that it cleanses and does not bite.
And guard yourself according to your virtue that the wound in your cure is clean; and there should not be in it surdicia or other; nor do you leave an aggregate of virulence in it; for when it is applied over the membrane of the brain, it corrupts that and causes that, and a malicious malignancy happens about the patient.
And it happens in the head of the head, as the bone is cleansed and most when the cure of it is lessened, and there is blackness on the surface of it; and therefore consider then if these accidents happen to the patient, know that it is all because of you.
And if blackness does not happen except from the medicine that is placed above it, and it is because of that medicine that makes that blackness, then take honey a part and rose oil parts .iii., and with those line with a cloth, and then place that on the head; and then cure that with the spices of cure which is convenient so that it is healed.
Chapter .iii.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two parts above most superior, or one of those some bent, because there are in it two bones, for the part below is cartilaginous, and is not broken and nothing happens to it except constriction and tortuosity and sinusitis.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then take your index finger and insert that into your nose, and press that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your thumb push out, so that the nose returns according to its natural figure. And let it be done by you with ease, and guard yourself so that pain does not occur to the patient because of that operation.
And if the fracture is in the highest part of the nose, and the finger does not reach that, then let it be split at its extremity where there is a little thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you make the appearance of that.
And bind yourself to restore it on the first day of the fracture if possible; and if not, then you must let the wound rest for two or three days until the pus of the cauterized wound is dried up.
And then insert in the extremity of the nose a ball of cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or put in two balls, if the fracture is in two parts, and let there be thickness in the ball of so much quantity that it fills up the hole of the nose.
And some of the old restorers say that the balls be wrapped in lamb, and changed every day, but I have not seen it; they say let the ball be wrapped in almond milk with comfit flour with powder of moly; and then leave it so that the bone is made firm and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes there is inserted into the nose, instead of the ball, pine resin more than that which is wrapped in cloth of lion; and let there be the fracture of the nose most violent and the patient not prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do that, and if you want, do balls.
And if another cauterized wound happens, plaster the nose with cerate or with cotton soaked in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if cauterized wound does not happen, then take flour of sesames and powder of onions made with almond milk and place that above it, and put over it lint of lion, and do not bind the nose by any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then split them and extract them with instruments that are convenient for that; and then close the cut, and cure that with what incarnates and hardens, and with plasters according to what is convenient.
And if a wound happens inside the nose, then cure that with balls and administer lead tubes so that it is completely healed.
Chapter .iiii.
Of restoration of the mandible when it is broken.
When the mandible below is broken and the fracture of it has no pus, regard then if the fracture is only outside, and is not broken in two and is bent to the sides, then its sense is obscure.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, then insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and similarly if the fracture is on the left mandible, then insert the index finger of the right hand; and drive with that the flexibility of the fracture inside with ease to the sides outside, with your other hand from the outside of the bone; thus you will bring it into a line.
If the jaw is already separated into two parts, then you should administer an extension of two parts according to what is right, so that it can be healed properly.
And if it happens that teeth are knocked out due to disruption, then strengthen those which you expect will remain, using gold or silver wire, or silk thread; and then apply a cerot to the broken jaw, and place a doubled-over drape on top, and place a large, decently-made plate, or a piece of wood equal in length to the jaw, on top of that; and then tie it all together according to how you are equipped to do so and according to what is convenient for the application, as long as it does not cause destruction.
And command the patient to rest and be still, and give him food in small amounts.
And if you think that the figure has been changed in any way, then on the third day, untie it and correct what is altered by applying a powder of linseed, egg white, or chickpea flour after removing the cerot, and place woolen cloth over it.
And then when the bandage is tight and adjusted over it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, so as to not untie it until the fracture is healed and strong; for many times this fracture is healed within three weeks.
And if it happens that an ulcer forms due to inflammation, then administer what we have said many times in the healing of it, so that the ulcer is healed and the swelling goes down.
And if the fracture is compound, then consider whether there is a piece of bone separated or protruding; then you should subtly apply pressure on those protrusions with what is convenient in the removal of those using instruments.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you should widen it with a bloodletting instrument according to the amount of your need.
And then when you have removed those protrusions and there is no inflammation remaining, you should close the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place one of the bandages that is convenient for that over it and let it heal.
Chapter 5.
On the restoration of the clavicle when it is dislocated.
Many times the clavicle on the front side of the chest is broken due to a blow by a muscle, and that fracture occurs in one of three ways.
For it may be broken, or separated into two parts without protrusions occurring; and it is easier to restore it; or it occurs with protrusions, and its restoration is more difficult; or the fracture is compound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is compound, is that you have two assistants, so that one holds the joint and follows the broken clavicle, and the other extends the collar bone towards that side.
And then join the fracture with the ligature threads so that it is made in the shape of a collar bone according to how it should be, and there is not a slight protuberance or concavity.
And if it needs more extension, then you should place below the patient's armpit a bandage or woolen cloth, and its size should be according to the amount of your need; and extend and elevate the clavicle and constrict the bandage with your hand so that it joins the fracture as it should.
And if you cannot pull the end of the clavicle towards the outside due to the chest being in the way, then the patient should be bent over at the neck, and place a cushion under their chest, and another cushion halfway down their back.
And the assistant should press the muscle towards the bottom ends so that the clavicle bone is elevated upwards from within, and then you should correct the fracture and join it with ligature threads.
But if you feel protrusions from the clavicle and they are swollen, then cut over them and extract those protrusions with ease.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you should make an incision over it with one of the instruments which you have equipped yourself for this purpose, after having placed beneath the clavicle the instrument that serves the head; and it is a wooden or iron instrument, of which this is the shape:
And it is like a chisel of which this is the concavity; and its width should be according to what you have need of it, according to the size of the bone or its weakness.
But its length should be according to what you are able to do in the operation; and it should have two ends, one wide and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound which you have incised when you have extracted the protrusion of the bone is wide, and there is no threat of an ulcer, then you should bring together the two lips of the wound with a suture.
And if the opening is small, or you fear an ulcer, then you should fill the wound with bandages and powders according to the amount of the opening of the wound.
And if it happens that an ulcer forms, then soak a bandage in warm vinegar and rose oil and place it over the ulcer.
And all of this should not be swollen over the broken bone; and therefore you should examine the patient every day, for when the binding is softened or you see the cloth already swollen, then you should correct that and tighten the binding.
And then place the patient' sleep on their chest, and place a cushion under their armpit from which they are sleeping at night, so that with that the support of them is raised from the side of it, and therefore the fracture of the collar bone is raised in elevation of the muscle; or their arm is bound to their neck.
And do not untie the binding if it does not happen in the place definitely or an ulcer forms, until after twelve days.
And then renew the bandage if you have seen it become loose, and tighten in compression and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the collar bone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
On the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula bone is broken at its broad end and not at its narrow end but at its edges.
And therefore when it is broken at its broad end — and is broken at its narrow end — and it is not dislocated but by contact, then according to the shape of the fracture being broken you should make the preparation of it and reduce it according to the natural figure in every way that it is possible for you.
And then apply a powder made of ground-up egg whites and stopped lion's teeth over the area, then sprinkle double-folded cloth over it, and finally place a thin layer of table salt on top, according to the amount of the spatula or a little more.
But if the salt has sunk into any of the spatula's indentations, then fill those indentations with stopped lion's teeth and level off the salt according to its equality.
And then strengthen it with a long, decently tight bandage and the firm placing of the salt so that it is not moved from its place.
And examine the bandage every day; and whenever it becomes soft, replace it with another bandage and tighten it and apply more salt if it has been moved from its place.
And lay the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in twenty days or less.
And so when this number is completed, then you must dissolve the bandage, and know that it is safe for the bones from which its beginning is not feared nor their dissolution.
And if the process of the bone is pressing on the spot and you put the chain underneath it, then you must cut into it, pierce it, and act according to what we have said in the fracture of the furcula of suppuration of pus if this happens here.
Chapter 7.
On the restoration of a broken foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle of it and this causes pain there, but its ends are usually crushed and broken down.
And in the accidents of it when its middle is broken, there is decline to the parts within and pain occurs in it, difficulty in walking, cough, strong fever and vomiting of blood; and a cavity is revealed in the broken bone and is not hidden from sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient be laid on his back, and a cushion placed between his shoulders; and then press upon the muscles of it, and adjust the ribs of it with the hands of two assistants, and apply pressure gently in the operation of it and in the adjustment according to all manner that is possible and convenient for him, so that the figure of the bone returns according to its nature.
And then apply plaster and gauze over it and place on top of it a thin layer of table salt or chalk or something similar in weight, after you wrap that in cloth; and then gently tighten it around the broken bone so that it is not moved; and sometimes press its back into the roundness of the back with decent pressure; and consider the bandage all the time, and when it becomes soft, tighten it.
And if necessity demands the removal of that which provokes pus or pricking, or pain, or suppuration that occurs in the spot, then you cut and loosen it and remove the plaster and rectify that which happens by means of its cure; and then reduce the plaster, if you see fit to do so, and adjust the pressure on him so that he is healed.
Chapter 8.
On the restoration of ribs when they are cracked.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not occur except in large spots that follow the spine, and from their ends to the front, it does not happen to them except those which are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from sensation because of the examination of it by the fingers.
And its restoration is that you close the fracture with the fingers according to the possible manner so that it is wrapped according to its nature, and then plaster and tighten the broken bone if pus comes from it.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining to the parts within, then there occurs in the patient violent pain and piercing, also as piercing that which occurs in him who has pleurisy, and because of it there is difficulty in breathing, cough, and vomiting of a lot of blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And the Antixs are born in her with sick children.
And from them are born: that which you pause for the patient who engenders inflammation and swollen condition, so that his belly may be inflated and extended, and the fracture is driven to the parts outside.
But we fear that for this there is not the cause to generate suppuration of pus if it does not occur; and if it does occur, then help it.
And some of them say that it is laid upon him wind and then that it sucks strength; and it is like reasoning, but nevertheless it is feared that the wind may throw out the superfluities to the spot because of weakness.
And some of them say: that the spot be covered with wool which has been submerged in hot oil, and that powdery bandages be placed between the ribs so that it may be filled, and then that the bandage is wrapped around him according to its roundness; and then let the patient be cured by means of poultice of food and medicine.
And if it causes violent constriction of the patient and he does not suffer, and the bone pierces his veil with piercing pain, we fear for the patient, then cut on the spot, uncover from the cracked rib, and then place upon it an instrument that conserves the phlegm, the memory of which is preceded; and pierce the bone with ease, and extract that.
And then let us adjust the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with cauterization, and cure it with bandages so that it may be healed.
And if suppuration occurs within it, then you cut and pour powders in rosin oil and place on the spot, and let him be cured by that which causes the suppuration within the teeth.
And let him lie on his side over which he slept so that it may be healed.
Chapter 9.
On the restoration of a fracture of the spine and collar bone.
Sometimes a fracture occurs in the bones of the collar, and they are crushed and broken down, and similarly to the vertebrae of the spine. And if this occurs to someone and you want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider the following: for if you see the fingers already soft and stupified and dead and cannot move them nor straighten them out, nor tighten them with your fingers or sting them with a needle without sensation, then you may know that it is not healed; and in the middle of their disposition it is deadly.
And if they are not soft but feel constriction in them with the fingers and piercing pain, then you may know that the nut of the bone is safe and that the patient will be cured by means of a cure.
And if you see the vertebrae of the collar like that and want to know whether it will be healed or not, then consider its feet.
Now if you see that they are already soft, it occurs to those which is occurred in the fingers, and then when he lies on his back, loses windiness and egestion according to his will, and sometimes is resupinated over his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then you may know that it is deadly, so do not trouble yourself with its cure.
But if none of these occurs, the cause is better and less dangerous.
And its cure is that you pierce with the needle of the suppuration of pus that you place on the cracked vertebra with hot rosin oil, or with seeds of houseleek; place upon it some times a day as long as the suppuration lasts.
And then apply one of the comforting and strengthening plasters to that area; and command the patient to rest and stay calm, and not to sleep on the side where he feels pain until he is healed.
And if it happens that the wound of the shoulder or any other cause has separated from him, then cover the wound and close the bone; and approximate the two lips of the wound with a suture, if it is large; and cure the wound with an application of a suitable ointment until it heals.
But if the rear of the shoulder bone is broken, and it is the end of the wound, then cover that you insert the little finger of the left hand in the anus, and grasp the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible by a second motion; and apply plaster and splints to it, if needed, and strengthen it.
And if you feel broken bones in him, then cover it and close it, and cure the wound with the cure that has preceded it until it is healed.
Chapter 10:
On the restoration of width.
The bones of the hips are often broken, and when they are broken, their fracture is nothing more than a separation at their ends, and they are sunken in length and decline to the inner parts; and it happens to the patient pain in the location and puncturing, and the leg is stupified when it is fractured.
And its restoration is that you extend your hand over it so that it is above the fracture as it is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is only at its end, then grasp it according to what appears to you by a motion of grasp so that it is assembled to its natural figure.
But if the fracture is in the length or that which declines to the inner parts below, then make the patient lie on his belly so that you are prepared for the restoration of that fracture. And therefore when you have grasped it, apply plaster to it, and place upon it a splint of wood or of gut, and strengthen it by compression over which you do not fear puncture of fracture nor removal of the splint.
And grasp the cavity with that which fills it so that it takes the compression according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or on his side.
But if it happens that there is suppuration of pus, then cease from extension of it and its restoration until the suppuration of pus is settled, and apply to it that which sedates that suppuration according to what has preceded; and then return to its restoration, and strengthen it thus.
And if it happens in the shoulder bones or are comminuted, then treat them.
Chapter 11:
On the restoration of the fracture of the elbow.
The elbow is what is between the arm pit and the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, then its restoration is in one of two manners, of which the one is that you take wood plank of moderate thickness according to this form:
And they are tied at their two ends with two lengths of rope, and it is suspended from the elevated location.
And make the patient lean on a stool; and then bend his broken arm over the wood so that it comes to the tip of it in the middle location from which is attached the curve of the wood.
And then have someone suspend over it some heavy cause, or the minister extend that to the inner parts below; and then the doctor and his assistants press the fracture with their hands, so that the fracture returns according as it should.
And the other manner is that the patient be reclined on his neck and suspend his arm from his shoulder with rope; and then command two ministers, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with his hands, and the other holds what is below him; and each of them extends to his side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then strengthen the fracture with rope and that which is above it with another rope, and each of the two ministers extend to their two sides.
And therefore if the fracture is near to the end of the muscle, then let half of the rope be paused over the tip and the other below the fracture from the arm pit.
And likewise if the fracture is near to the arm pit, then let the rope be paused over that location and over that arm pit.
Grasp that with facility and without pain, and then when it is grasped according as it should and is applied by good application, then strengthen that if suppuration of pus does not happen.
But if suppuration of pus happens, then cease the compression until the seventh day, and apply to it wool cloth dipped in wine and rose oil so that the suppuration rests, and then strengthen it.
And the manner of strengthening that is that you apply plaster to the fracture, and then place wrapping of new cloth over the plaster; and then adjust the arm upon that elbow, and pause your open hand over his muscle.
And pause the cloth and strengthen the elbow of the arm so that the arm is in place of splints, if not prohibited by anything of the broken bone.
And then use splints.
And it is that you pause upon the fracture that splint which is more loaded and stronger of the other splints; and pause between each splint the breadth of a finger, and let the length of the splints be according to the fracture with addition of three fingers of all parts.
And then strengthen over the splints with the compression which was said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is that your compression over the location of the fracture be more strict. And when the fracture is lengthened, then let the compression be less.
And therefore if you see the placement of the splints and the compression thus as we have said in the hour in which you restore the member to its position, then do.
And if you fear suppuration of pus, then cease the compression and the splints until the seventh day as we have said.
And then consider the rope in all parts for three days so that no pain comes in the location or inflammation; or is prohibited food coming to the member by disposition of superfluity of compression; and then rectify all that according as we have said.
But if you are over confidence that nothing of that happens, then do not cease the ligature except after three days or more.
And let his sleep be on his back, and his hands be over his stomach.
And pause over the elbow woolen cloth wound around it, and consider throughout the hour of the night or day that it is not loosed nor dissolved the figure of the broken member, or that the rope be molified, then rectify all that with your skill.
And pause the food of the patient according to the order which we have given before, so that the food be subtle in the beginning so that it is begun strengthening; and then let it grow larger.
The custom of the elbow and of his arm is that they are bound for xl days; and then let them be loosed and a bath be administered and it be medicated with ointments which rectify that.
And if the fracture is healed, then do not cease from that the rope and the splints until a day or two months.
Chapter 12:
On the restoration of a broken arm.
The arm consists of two bones which are called two rays; one of which is small and it is that which is located at the tip of the fingers, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small one, from the part further down.
And very often only the greater ray is broken, or the smaller one, and very rarely are they both broken at the same time.
And when the smaller ray is broken above, its restoration is easier and its healing quicker.
And when it's broken below, its fracture is more serious and its healing more difficult.
And the worst of all is when both bones are broken at the same time.
Therefore, if the bone which is broken is the smaller one above, then it is convenient for the doctor to put a small extension with ease into that area so that it can be splinted.
But if it's the greater ray which is broken, then he should put a more forceful extension.
And if both rays are broken at the same time, then you must put on the splint with the most strength.
And you must put the hand position for its restoration and extension spread out on a pillow, with the palm of the hand to the upper parts, more above all the others fingers, and the auricular finger lower than the other fingers, and the patient lying on his back on a raised pillow.
And there should be a pillow beneath him in the height or angle of his body, so that he does not suffer pain.
Then the assistant extends the arm from the lower part with his hand or with a bandage, and another assistant extends above in the same way.
And then the doctor splints the bone so that it is reduced according to the best possible figure.
And if in the structure of the bone there are knots, then you must reduce all pressure on it with your strength.
And if it appears on him separated knots which push the tendon and you don't have hope in its restoration, then you must operate on it and stop that according to the manner we've said above.
But if the fracture is with pus, I have already made a chapter about that in particular, and so take the cure of that.
And if an abscess comes at the beginning of your restoration, then you must line a cloth with cerot made of rose oil and white wax, and it should be medium between thickness and thinness, and stretch the cloth tightly over that abscess so that the abscess yields; and then remove the cerot and apply plaster prepared from powdered mulberry wood with honey, and then put on poultices; and the poultice which is put over that fracture should be larger and stronger.
And know that the number of the arm's stars are seen in the major dispositions, or whether it is a fracture in one ray or in both rays at the same time.
And then put your splint over the area of the fracture more strongly and more forcefully, and if you see more swelling at the upper parts of the fracture or at its lower parts, put on the splint a little more loosely according to how it is preceded by its remembrance in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths which are wrapped over the fracture should be soft and moistened, and not hard or rough, and the thread with which it is tightened should be of a medium between thin and thick, just as we have recommended.
And consider the member and the bandage again after a few days. And if any cause happens which necessitates a correction, just as it happens to the member, and then you must bathe the member with warm water so that that swelling stops.
And leave the member not constricted while he rests, and then return the splint.
And if the splint is already loosened and the bone is healed and like those, then correct all that with your strength.
And consider also if the nourishment is prohibited from coming to the member because of the excess of the splint, then you must loosen that a little and leave it for a few days so that it runs to that member, and then tighten it.
But if to the patient no happens any cause of those we've said, then there is no need for it to be loosed within about 20 days or more of those.
And then the hand of the patient should be suspended by its power from motion, and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 days or in 32 days, or very often it is restored in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complications and dispositions of strength.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of the fracture of the wrist and fingers.
Know that in the wrist and knuckles of the fingers there is a certain fracture, and there does not happen to them except many times repeated.
And when there happens to the hand fracture or sprain, then the patient should lie on his back and his hands be spread according to equality, and then put his hand over that extended one.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones and the doctor splints them so they are aligned with good alignment.
And then you must put plaster and lint if there is no abscess, and then put a poultice over that according to the quantity of the place, and you have already wrapped it in a moist cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers towards the belly of the palm, then make a bandage like a tourniquet, and command the patient to tighten that with his palm fractured; then it should be tightened with a long cloth.
And the cloth of the body with which it is lifted so that the body is applied to the palm; and tighten that according to how it should be.
But if the fracture is towards the outer parts, then you must put a poultice over that and another poultice over the part below in the palm so that the hand is open and straight; and then put on the splint, just as the hand is turned to the side between the fingers with bandages.
And if there is fracture in any of the fingers of the fingers, then if it's the finger that pushes, it should be splinted according to how it should be and then it should be tightened with the palm and if you want, put a small poultice straight so that the fracture is raised and not bent.
But if the fracture is in the other fingers, just as in that of the middle, or in the index, or in the auricular, or in the ring finger, then it should be splinted and tied with the finger which touches it; or they are all tied according to continuation, and it is better; or put a small poultice over that, just as we've said about the pusher.
And consider in your restoration and then that, the disposition of abscess cauterization — by the operation of it — by what it is repeated his narration.
Chapter 14.
On the restoration of the fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The bone of the thigh many times is broken and is revealed to feeling according to how it is turned towards the front and to the upper part.
And its restoration is done so that the bandage is tightened over the fracture and another bandage below the fracture, and the patient lies on his back and on his face.
And then the minister extends all the ligament to its end according to its quality when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is near the root of the head, then the ligament near the nostril should be extended to the part of the penile bone, in order that the extension may draw it upwards, and the other ligament below the fracture.
And likewise if the break is near the nostril, then the ligament near the nostril should be extended and the extension to the parts below.
And then the doctor should reduce the bone with both hands until it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bones are joined by a good union; then he should apply an ointment and a tight bandage if there is no sign of inflammation in the limb. But if there is inflammation in it, then you should leave it for several days until the inflammation subsides, and then return to your cure.
The bandage should be applied like this: fold a hard bandage over the fracture two or three times and soak up the excess fluid; then wrap the leg so that it reaches from the foot to the root of the head.
And insert long feathers between the head and the leg near the nostril to the parts below; then the ends of the feathers should come from the highest part of both parts, and then you should wrap around the leg and head what is left of the bandage.
And then place a splint over the fracture on the head and another splint over the bone of the leg.
And then fill the spaces between the leg and the head with soft rags so that the bandage is coagulated.
And then begin with a strong bandage over the fracture using three or four bandages; and as you go further from the fracture site, your bandage should be weaker, longer, and softer.
And then extend both ends of the feathers to the parts below until it reaches the ankle of the foot; and tighten both ends of the splints on the other side so that the ligament is not pushed from its place.
And then let the bandage be over that in such a way that there is no sign of inflammation or suppuration or swelling in the limb; and if any of those things occur, then you should loosen it, and correct what has occurred according to what we have said several times.
But if there is an injury to the bone that you can reduce, then you should reduce it according to what is possible for you; and if not, leave it and treat it with what has been mentioned before so that it heals.
And in some cases this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with splints on both sides as we have said for the arm and the forearm.
However, when it is restored in this way there is no sign of limping in the patient; and if it is restored alone, then it is necessary that the other leg limp as well.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a little more or less, and all of that is according to the variety of the complications and other dispositions.
Chapter 15
Of the restoration of the fracture of the nostril.
Know that the fracture of the nostril is sharp and deep; but it happens to it many times tension.
But if there is a fracture, then it is not more than a crack or cut in its parts; and that is on the skin or under the skin, and over that all is covered by a membrane.
And its restoration is such that what is broken in its parts is reduced by the fingers until it is aggregated and joined according to what is possible by action, facility, and fabrication.
And then apply an ointment and place a round splint over it if necessary; and tighten it above with a bandage as is convenient.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have mentioned in other fractures; as suppuration occurs and likewise to those in which there is no sign of inflammation, doing to it whatever is convenient for its healing.
Chapter 16
Of the restoration of the fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is large and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is slender and is called the shin.
And it happens to both kinds of fracture what happens in the arm; and its restoration is such that the restoration of the arm is done by reduction, and the operation of it is one.
And if both bones are broken together, then the leg is split into all parts.
And if only the more slender bone is broken, then the leg is split in front.
And if only the larger bone is broken, then it is bent below and is of those causes which are not under your control.
And then administer extension and action and liaison of the splints according to that reduction.
However, if the fracture of the leg is oblique having many splinters, then let the extension be less and weaker, and draw it away with your strength.
And if there is any cause for an operation on the leg, and when you reduce the splints and complete all your operation, then you shall take lengths of pine wood which have been paused below the causes which have been paused in soliders, and made evenly with white poplar; lengthen them so that they are small, not a lot of them, and not be a finger-width apart, and their length according to the length of the leg from the front to the back.
And then wrap around each one in two bandages according to the length of it; and pause one over the part of the leg from one side and the other from the other side, and let them be from the front to the back at the bottom of the ankle of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places from both ends and at the middle point; by this bandage the leg is prohibited from bending to the right and left side, and stands in good position.
And then a wooden brace according to the length of the leg is administered; and let the leg be paused in it so that it conserves its movement.
And most importantly you shall do this in fracture, and when that is suppurating properly.
And then consider the leg every day and study it with final study, and guard it from suppuration and inflammation and other similar causes; and if any of those things occur, then you shall treat it with what is convenient for its healing.
Chapter 17
Of the fracture of the ankle and the bones of the foot and the toes.
The ankle does not suffer fracture at all; but the bones of the foot suffer fracture in some ways; and the toes of the foot suffer fracture seldom, and do not suffer except by accident during the time of disposition.
And if the bones of the foot are broken and those bones are raised on each other, then let the patient rest his foot on the ground prepared thus with a pillow.
And then pause one foot over those bones and press them and reduce them into their places; and when they are reduced, then twist them in their places.
Then apply a plaster and a bandage on top of it, and place below the belly of the foot a small board with two holes in it.
Then stretch that leg down while you have wrapped it in clothes and other materials that cause heat.
And when three or four days have passed, then you should loosen the bandage, for you will find that the bones are swollen, or broken or displaced, and the operation on them is as follows.
But if anyone says that they are broken, then restore them and join them together as I described to you in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. But if what is broken of the bones is two or three or more, then you should place on each finger a splint of its size wrapped in lion's cloth; and stretch the board below the foot, and let it stand on one of the board's holes so that it has good retention.
And it is convenient for you not to forget that you do not consider all the causes that are given to you in other fractures of the accidents that we have said; and pay attention to any accident with what covers it.
Chapter .xviii.
Of fracture of the pubic bone and of the bone of the penis and of the woman's clitoris.
When the pubic bone is broken, then make that woman lie on her back, and tilt her pelvis slightly to one side, and let it be held there.
