And let powder be placed on her back, and when she wants to urinate let the cotton be moistened with facilidade, and let her urinate, and reduce it and return in the same way as before, and let her return to her first powder.
And do this for seven days or around that, for it is restored.
And if you want, take a bladder of sheep and stretch over the wound a catheter of wool; and insert the whole bladder into the pubic bone, and let it be insufflated through the catheter with air so that the bladder is inflated inside the bone, for the fracture returns.
And let it be filled with spermaceti and stay there for a few days as we have recommended so that it heals.
And when the bone of the penis of a man or woman is broken, then administer in its restoration and piercing what we have recommended for the bone of the hip.
And that which is straight does not hide in these nearby fractures and which few understand, especially those who have some study and understanding of this my book; for that fracture instructs you on the major dispositions through a direct path in its restoration and stricture, and therefore understand well.
But in the clitoris of a man when it is broken, take a whetstone of ash, and place the clitoris in that; then place on it a binding of cloth, and let it be bound, and let it be left for three days or around that time, and let it heal.
Chapter .xix.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the bones when there is pus.
It is convenient for those to whom fractures happen with pus, especially if the bone is large like the bone of the thigh or the adductor, or similar to that, that you take care and phlebotomize them as soon as the conditions of phlebotomy testify to you, as we have said above.
And if the pus flows out with blood, then understand that you take care to retain it there as you pulverize on it dried triphylite, unless another cause is present.
And let the restoration of the fracture begin in that same day and do not prolong it if an abscess does not happen.
But if an abscess happens, then let the restoration of it be left until the sixth day, so that the abscess has ceased; and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, for you will cause harmful accidents.
But if the broken bone is prominent over the uncovered tendon, then understand that you pierce it and reduce it by joining them with your hands with ease and little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the equation of it by your hands, then let it be reduced by your hands with this instrument; and the instrument which is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight digits and whose breadth is according to the amount of pus, and it is necessary for the physician to take one of those three or four according to the amount that occurs in the cure in all kinds of fractures.
And let it be round and have little thickness so that it is not bent by the pressure upon it in the hour of the operation.
And let its tip be acute to which the edges are attached; and let it be of thick thickness above, and of thin thickness within at the ends.
And this is the shape of it:
And it is called in Greek "labrys vlantz ate-larem", and it is a little palette.
So that the acute end of it is placed elevated over the prominent end of the bone.
And press upon it in a wet way so that the bone returns, and let it be joined with some equation.
And therefore try to join the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of it broken is thin, and do not take the instrument for a good perspective, then understand that you insert the end of that bone so that the instrument has power over it.
But if you cannot reduce the bone in any way we have recommended, then insert it with whatever is convenient for it from the incision points we have said; or saw it with one of the saws in whatever way is possible for you.
And let what rises in the bone of roughness and of small scars be shaved off.
And when the bone is reduced and especially the reduction causes violent pain in the wound, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; therefore do this which gives great benefit to the patient.
When your restoration which you do to the bone is completed, and especially let the dressing be submerged in black styptic wine, and properly if it is summer, and do not place on the pus wax nor another cause of which oil is made so that no fever and corruption happen in it.
And let splints be administered in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone and let the pus be uncovered so that you insert the wound with forceps and use bandages according to the amount of the pus.
And guard yourself from all carelessness so that you do not tighten the pus with the fracture, for many times this has been done by foolish physicians and they caused on their patients death or corrosion or putrefaction, therefore it is a fault. If his tightness was lighter, softer, different from the tightness of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or is a bad wound, and you fear over that certain bad accidents which we have mentioned, and you feel pain in the place, then do not cover over that with bandages, but rather make a wrapping of hard cloths in place of bandages and tighten him with those.
And when after a day or two the wound begins to heal, and pus has formed in it, then you take off the cloth which you placed over that, and you apply poultices with which according to our custom we treat wounds, like tetratapharmacon and similar to that.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the ligature and examine the wound every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and that the member be prepared in such a way that the pus runs off from it with ease.
If the wound passes many days and is not consolidated nor is the pus discharged from it, then know that here are some small things because of which one must probe the wound with a tent. And of those things which are separated and extracted, those which are not separated and put in the member and cause pain, pierce them as is possible for you to do.
And if it happens that the wound becomes swollen or turns yellow, there is stoma, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction, then you must treat each one with what is fitting for it according to its cure, of which a narrative precedes in its chapter.
And about this on which I will dwell and you must weigh your decision: when the bones are broken, they protrude from the member like the bones of the hip and the helper, and similarly to those of the large members, and you must not presume attraction or extraction of it, nor the extraction of it many times happened by which death; leave that alone so that pus can flow out, for often it will fall on its own after about 20 or 30 days; and then cure the wound if you see it, and if not, leave that.
Chapter 20
Of the cure of a lump which happens in the path of any fracture.
Many times this lump happens in the path of healing of a fracture, and mostly it is near the joints, and because of it the figure of the member is altered; and often it prohibits the member from its natural operation.
Therefore consider if the lump is recent and administer in that medicine which has tightened like aloe, and myrrh, and mastic, and cress, and similar to those; so you take some or all of those, and mix them with wine vinegar, or with white wine, and place that on the lump on a piece of cloth, and tighten that around it with good constriction.
And leave the constriction so that you do not loosen it for many days; and then loosen it and repeat another, while the lump is softened; or tighten over that a sheet of lead descendingly, because lead has property of softening everything which is swollen in the members.
But if the lump is already turned to stone and it is hard and necessity forces you to cut that tightly, then split that through the part which is most above it, and pierce the superfluity and protruding, or cut that with any resector until it is softened; and cure that until it is healed.
Chapter 21
Of restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then the member becomes subtle and frail not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the member becomes subtle and frail, then that is not made except for many causes: of which one cause is multitude of swelling of the ligature and of the binding of it made not as it should; or by superfluity of constriction of the ligaments within which the nourishment is prohibited from running to the member; or by multitude of superfluous embrocation; or by superfluous movement not in its hour; or by weakness of blood in the body of the patient and because of frailty of him.
And cure of that is to make the patient eat and to make his heart tender so that blood is multiplied in him; and administer a bath; and put cow's milk and leticia on it.
And then place plaster on the member, so that the plaster draws to it much nourishment, or continue embrocation of it with lukewarm water, while the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter 22
Of cure of the broken bones when they are restored tortuously and prohibit the operation of them from what they should do.
When it happens to the member already restored in the healing of its tortuosity and eminence to the broken bone and lump, and it is because of the form of the member, but however the member is not prohibited from its natural operation, therefore you must not take the word of one who examines that member as though it were newly broken.
But already many foolish doctors and restorators have done that in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy, presenting great fear whose sign is deadly.
But if claudication and lump are recent, then let them be embrocated with lukewarm water in which soft herbs are cooked, like leaves of alder and roots of it, and mallow, and similar to those; and let an ointment be made from it with soft-making oil; or let it be moistened with mucilage of root of alder and be cooled with cotton wool or with linseed oil, and let it be plastered with that; or let them be applied fat figures and tied with the feathers of a pigeon, and similar to those of the medicines which are named brother's consolidator.
And sometimes it is dissolved by continual gentle friction which is made with the hands moistened, and let the member be moved throughout in all hours.
But if the tortuosity is gone in front and is made strong and turned to stone and caused necessity of cure of it by iron, then let the pus be above dried up and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and let that which is superfluous of lump of that bone with subtile incisors, and let facility be administered in that with solicitude and study.
Chapter 23
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is displacement of one of the joints of its place, by which it is prohibited from movement, and the figure of the member is altered, and brings upon the patient great pains and injuries.
Therefore when it happens to any dislocation, then let it be reduced as quickly as possible, and do not delay its reduction, because if it is delayed, the joint is apoplectic and very difficult to reduce dislocation.
And for that reason do not let it be softened nor stretched in the hour of apoplexy, because many times it brings on spasms and pains.
But when that happens, then let you take care of the phlebitis of the patient, and then let it be left so that the apoplexy rests; and then the member is embrocated with tepid water and oil, and then let it be reduced with ease.
And each member is cured according to that of which the reminder will come in its place.
And you have arranged the chapters of dislocation according to that which is preceded in fracture, from the uppermost part of the body towards the lowermost part.
Chapter XXIV.
Of the cure of dislocation of the jaw below.
The two jaws are dislocated if they do not grind their teeth; and the dislocation of both occurs in one of two ways: either they are slightly loosened from their joints by a slight movement, and are slightly softened; or they are completely dislocated and fully completed, while they are softened at the beginning of the joint between them, so that saliva runs from the patient's mouth and he cannot close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his speech.
But when the dislocation of him is slight, then in the best dispositions it returns by itself for some slight cause.
But if the dislocation is complete and full, then it must be administered quickly, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister holds the head of the patient, and the doctor inserts his right finger into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the patient's mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or he inserts his two fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others hold his hands outside which grip; and command to the patient that he cease his jaws and release them from every part; and the doctor grips the jaw and splints it until it returns to its place.
And if it is difficult to reduce him, and especially if both jaws are dislocated, then administer evaporation with water and oil, while the reduction of both is made easier and not delayed at all, as we have said.
And when they return and are bound, and the mouth of the patient is closed and not softened, then place on them a powder of linen with cerotum, and let it be made of wax and rose oil; and let it be bound easily with soft ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be standing between two pillows, so that he does not grind his teeth to the right or left; and do not force him to chew anything for any cause; let his food be lion's milk, while when the pains are loosened and the jaws are sore, then he grinds what he wants.
And let this be administered easily, and do not labor over the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and resting, while the jaw is sore and healed.
And if it is difficult to reduce two jaws when they are dislocated at any time and do not return to their places, then many times a fever comes suddenly and there is a continuous heat, and the belly of the patient is very hot, and very often he vomits pure bile.
And when you see this, then you know that it is perilous; and many times he dies within ten days who has this.
Chapter XXV.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the end of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the side within through its continuity with the foot; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer end, and this is indicated by sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient lies on his back and extends his arms. Then press the joint with your palm with strong compression, for it will return.
And let a plaster and powder be placed on it and strengthen that.
But the end of that which is connected to the shoulder and continued from it, is not dislocated on any day; then let it be reduced and bound as we have said and as your apparatus is prepared.
And let a plaster, powder, and bandage be placed on it, and command the patient to adhere to tranquillity and rest until he is healed.
And with this medical treatment, the end of the muscle is reduced when separated from its place.
Chapter XXVI.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the end of the breastbone towards the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the top of the muscle, and this is done tightly; and it is not dislocated at the sides by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated in front by the nerves.
And especially it is dislocated and exits below at the ends of the breastbone, and especially in those whose flesh is thin, it is dislocated easily; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it exits out with difficulty.
And very often occurs in some men a blow or a fall, and the muscle of him is apoplectic from apoplexy, and it is swollen because of this, although he is already dislocated.
Therefore, you must examine this while you are on top of him, and then proceed in his treatment.
And the dislocation of it is known when it is at the ends below the breastbone; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the normal muscle, for you will see manifest difference among them; and you will feel in the head of the muscle concavity and above the breastbone from the touch of the head of the muscle as a hollow, and to the patient cannot reduce his hand to his shoulder, nor move that by any movement.
If it is dislocated behind or inside, and you then feel manifest touch, and it is not hidden.
And the reduction of this dislocation is made easier when it is fresh and the patient is an infant.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the hand of him to the ends above; and let the attendant place his hands under the breastbone of him, and raise the joint with strength into its place, and the minister raises the hand of him, and extends it above; and let him then lower that below so that it returns easily.
But if by what we have said it does not return, and it has started from the beginning that the dislocation occurred, many days, then let the patient be bathed in warm water and be administered fomenting which softens and loosens as though his joints were cut with a razor, and fenugreek, and honey in water, and let it be administered.
And then let the patient lie on his back and let him extend his arm below the breastbone of him, and wait with wool that it returns between elevation and compression; and let the doctor hold his shoulder, and raise the head of the muscle with strength, and pull the hand of the patient so that he does not grind his teeth at the ends, and let the other minister hold the head of the patient, so that he does not grind his teeth at the ends, for then it will return to its place.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this manner: and it is that you make present one man who is longer than the patient, and let him be on the patient's side, and let him insert his muscle under the breastbone of the patient and raise the breastbone of him above while the patient is suspended in the air, and let the other minister pull the hand of the patient from the ends of his belly. But if the patient is light, then let him who for another cause be suspended, so that it is heavy, for then the dislocation will return at once.
And it is reduced likewise according to another manner: and it is that a long post be placed on the ground whose head is round in shape, not thick nor slender, and then let him who is under the patient; let cloths be placed on the head of the post, and let the patient stand according to the length of the post, and then let his hand be extended from the ends of the other side with strength, for then the joint will return to its place quickly.
If it is difficult to reduce it for all the reasons that we have mentioned, then use this treatment: Take a stick whose length is two cubits, and whose breadth is four digits, and whose thickness is removed according to the curve of the breastbone in this way.
And then place a round cloth over the head so that the stick does not hit the patient.
And then the stick is placed below the breastbone, and the hand or arm is extended over the stick between the parts below, and the stick is tied over the buttock and the arm and the end of the hand.
Then the arm is placed on a ladder according to its breadth, and the hand is extended to the parts below, and the other side is suspended from the other side so that the joint will quickly come into alignment.
And when the instrument of it is completed according to any manner that you are able, then cover that which you have placed below the breastbone with a woolen cloth of equal quantity; and then place plaster prepared from ground black pepper and egg white over all the muscle, also rolled up above, and then tightly tie down the breastbone with a tight bandage; and let the ligament be rolled over the plaster, and the hand of it be attached to his neck, and let him be left, and may his hand not be hurt for four days.
And see that the food of the patient is light while the member is being eased, for that is better for his healing.
And then let food be given more often seven or eight times and exercise be taken; and therefore if it is stiff and not soft, then it has been healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to moisture that happens to it or due to another cause, then let three cauterizations be administered in it, according to what was said before in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all this is done and then seven days later the ligament is loosened and the joint is not closed up, and the plaster and bandage are removed from it several times, and it is not closed up and cats cannot be lifted to the parts above by it, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are cut or stretched out or made soft, and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of that which is dislocated from the foot and breast or the parts behind is made by pushing and extending with hands while returning it to its place.
And let the other bandage be administered in it, and cure that until it is healed.
And if healing becomes heavy in the member and delay in its movement happens, then let the patient take baths many times until that heaviness is relieved and he returns to his first nature.
Chapter .XXVII.
On the curing of dislocation of the neck.
Know that the joint of the neck is dislocated with difficulty and returns to its place with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all parts, and most to the front part and behind, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because when you adjust the neck dislocated with its head, that is clearly stated to you, for the joint is hollowed out and cannot bend the arm or touch with it his own muscle.
And see that you reduce the dislocation before it comes to him with an aposteme cauterized, because if it comes to him with an aposteme cauterized, its reduction is difficult and he will not be healed from it entirely; and especially when it is to the back parts, for it is worse than all other kinds of dislocations, and causes more pain, and often brings about death.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce it, is for the hand of him to be extended with two hands, and his arm expanded, and the hands of the doctor being on the neck and underneath him, and he pushing the joint with his fingernails or with the edge of his palm while returning it to its place.
But if the dislocation is to the front parts, then he returns so that he bends his hand with his own hand, while cutting the neck with the edge of his palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer violent extension and very strong pressure.
And it is that two ministers expand the arm, and hold that joint so that it is not bent due to extension.
And then let the arm be rolled over all parts, more than it is wrapped in a folded cloth on the hand of him, or a ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, anoint it with some oil so that it is not caused by any causes which help in the lubrication of the joint with ease; and then push the joint with violent pushing while returning it to its place.
And then its reduction let it be placed on that plaster in which it is tightened and dried with egg white, and let it be loosened with a decent bandage, and the arm be suspended from his neck and let him be left for some days.
And then let it be loosened; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then you loosen his ligament and leave that.
And if you see the joint not be strong, then you iterate the plaster and the ligament, and leave that for some days until it is loosened; and then loosen that.
But if it happens to him heaviness due to the reduction of the joint and its disposition is in movement, then administer moistening in a bath and subtle friction, and compression while being loosened; or place over the joint aleliati, which is the oil of the fat of a lamb; and then let it be bound and leave that on the bone for the day and night. And then you wash that and put him in a bath; and when he is warm, then you rub the joint with temperate friction, and then iterate over that aleliati which is the oil of rosemary, once daily for three days. Corruption has already occurred in the brain's interior instrument due to the bath, for it is limited.
And if you wish, place over that the udder of a cow which is moist and warm with butter and strewn over it; and do that several times, for it is moist and returns to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
On the curing of dislocation of malaccen, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Malaccen of the hand some times is dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easier, and the contrary is of other joints; however if there is swiftness in its reduction in the hour that it is dislocated, before the place becomes aposteme cauterized or that an aposteme occurs in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and the minister expands his hand, and the doctor places his hand or the palm of his hand over the imminence of the joint, and push it while returning to its place.
However if it is considered that the palm is already directed to the belly of the hand, then let the patient place both of his hands on a table due to extension and reduction; and if the dislocation comes before the two hands, then let the position of his hand be intrinsecally over the table so that the hand of the doctor may lean over that imminence of the joint.
And if he returns to normal immediately, that is good; and if not, then strengthen that area with a plaster, and let the blister remain; and do not repeat this, for otherwise you will not be able to endure the reduction of it, and more days will pass.
But examine the joint according to its tortuosity; and do not tell the patient any cause, unless your hand is made soft and you cannot strengthen it; then know that the nerves are cut or torn, and in this case there is nothing wrong with him except that he must be strengthened by cautery near his wound, and he should be made strong in this regard.
Therefore when the patient is returned, then apply to him the plaster which we have recommended, and let it remain and be strengthened for several days; and then let it be loose and let it remain, and let his hand be exercised.
But if his movement is impeded and some gravity occurs in him, then loosen that area with water and some rubbing several times, until he is eased.
Chapter 29.
Of the cure of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated entirely.
And when any of these fingers is dislocated towards the second or third part of the palm, then you should press and push back the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns to normal.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend that part towards which it is dislocated, and let it be for two days.
And then suspend and extend that within the day until it is straightened out.
And when night comes, then ligature that according to the narration, and do not stop loosening it during the day or exercising it by moving it, and let it be thus for several days until it is straightened out.
And do this also if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; and ligature that part and do your first operation on it, until it is healed.
And similarly, you should do with the others dislocations.
Chapter 30.
Of the cure of dislocation of the vertebrae of the back.
When complete dislocation occurs on any of the vertebrae of the back or neck and many vertebrae are removed from their places, then in this there is no cure, because death comes to the patient.
And the sign of this is that the excretion of the patient is outside his will, and he cannot retain it; and many times some of his members or both of his legs or both of his arms are made soft, or one of these; or a vertebra is displaced from its place, because many times it is displaced and the displacement of these is to four places.
Therefore, when it is displaced to the back parts, it is called hunchbackedness.
But its cure is that then you should consider whether the hunchbackedness occurs from childhood, because then there is no cure for it and it has no complete healing at all.
But in that which comes from accident or concussion or similar to these, the Ancients have said their ways of cure with prolix speech, of which the most returns with profit; but I have already abridged and abbreviated that for the benefit of my hearer because of my proximity to my understanding and by which I have explained it and demonstrated it with diverse instruments.
And those are not healed except that which occurs properly in the back by what I have related.
And let the patient be extended on his face over a pillow so that his feet are not elevated.
And then a straight staff is placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the pillow around the head of him; and another staff is placed in the ground under his feet at the other end of the pillow.
And the minister should hold the staff, and it should not be fixed in a hole; and another minister should hold the other according to that form.
And then a bandage is wrapped around the head of the patient and under it the tongue of the lion is pressed tightly; and extend the end of the tongue to the staff which is under the head of him, and let that be in him; and then tighten the other bandage over his buttocks by his fingers, and let them be under his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and let them be in the other staff which is under his feet.
And then each minister should extend the bandage to the end of the staff, and let the two staffs not be displaced from their places in which they are fixed, but rather not be fixed as we have said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the vertebra with strength until it returns to normal, or let him lie on a table, and then let him adjust his feet on the table until he returns to normal.
But if it does not return in this way, then take a table of which the breadth or length is three yards long, and hollow out in the wall from which we have said it is near the patient a place into which extends the end of the table. And then let the middle of the table be placed over the vertebra; and the doctor should place his feet on the other end, and stretch tightly until the vertebra is contracted and returns to its place.
And if you want to, open with a windlass which is turned by hand; and let there be fixed in the ground two staffs of one yard long, of which the length between each staff is one palm, and there is made in each staff a hole in which the windlass turns; and each staff is fixed in the ground so that it is not displaced.
And there shall be a round staff in which is the windlass, and let it be turned in the two holes of the two staffs.
And at its end there is a hole in which the staff is formed, the length of which is one palm as it turns; and similarly in the other two staffs.
And then tighten the bandages which you have tightened on the feet of the patient in the windlass which is under his head; and tighten the bandages which you have tightened on his buttocks in the turning which is under his feet.
And then each turn and let a minister twist the turn by hand; and the doctor smoothens the vertebra as we have said.
And when the vertebra has returned to normal and the place is smoothed out, then let there be put over it a plaster made of white vinegar; and then let there be stopped with cloth; and then let him lie on the table of which the breadth is three yards or around that, and the length of it is the quantity which takes up the hunchbackedness, and over the place of the vertebra.
And then let him be bound with the bandage we have said, and the patient be given subtle food until he is healed.
And if some swelling remains in the place at the end of the healing, then let there be administered medicines which moisten and lubricate by administration of the table for a long time, which we have related; and in that place lead sheet has been administered.
And sometimes they will suffer from an imminence in the lower part of the spine, and it is considered to be due to that dislocation; and it is this bone which is affected by this medicine, and therefore you must not apply it again, as this could lead to their death very quickly.
Chapter 31.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscles are not intended for dislocation alone, but only occur in those which occur in other joints with slight displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways: it is dislocated internally or externally, or it can be dislocated forward or backward.
And it is mostly dislocated internally, and rarely dislocated forward or backward.
And a sign of its dislocation to the front is that when you straighten the patient's leg, it will be longer and more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot backwards, and there will be an apostemata around the hip joint from the cautery, indicating that the head of the hip joint has moved here.
And the sign of which one it occurs to the back is that its accidents are contrary to those listed above.
And the sign that it occurs to the front is that they straighten their leg according to the compliment, but still cannot bend that foot due to pain; and if they try to walk on tiptoe, they cannot; and their urine is retained and they have apostemata around the joint, and they walk on their heels.
And the sign that it occurs to the back is that they cannot straighten their leg or bend it forward due to pain; and their leg is shorter than the other, and the joint is softened, and the head of the hip joint has moved backward.
But the reduction of this dislocation is that you should consider whether it is old and ancient, having been like that for a long time, that it will not be healed by simply reducing it and setting it in place; therefore, no medicine should be applied to it.
But for the one in which it is recent, and it is one of the four types of dislocation, then you should move the joint and extend it to the front and back, and from the right to the left, as this will quickly return and not require any further treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then you should prepare to pull and extend the leg from the lowest part, either with their own hands or with a strap placed over the leg above the joint; and another person should hold the top of the leg below the patient's fingers while pulling upward, and another should press the lion-like strap against the root of the hip.
And it is an extension from the front to the armpit, or from the back to the spine.
And they should both be extended in one movement while the patient is raised from the ground and their leg hangs down.
And this type of extension applies to all four types.
So if the dislocation returns as we have said, all is well; but if not, then it is necessary to use any means possible to achieve the cure.
In the proper reduction of it when it is dislocated and the dislocation is to the front, the patient should lie on their back; then place the strap over the root of the hip at the point between the head of the hip joint and the part below the armpit; and another person extends the strap above the armpit to the highest point of the collarbone.
And another person should grab the patient's arms and press the hip joint strongly while another pulls outward violently, as this will return it to its proper place.
And this is the quickest cure by which that part is reduced more quickly than with other methods.
But if you are not able to do this and cannot persuade them to use this method of cure for all parts, then at least tie both the patient's feet together with a strong strap, quickly, on their heels and toes.
And the length of each part should be four digits, and the leg with the dislocation should be extended more than the healthy one by two digits.
And the patient's leg with the dislocation should be suspended from a beam above their head, and they should be in the middle position; and its length from the ground should be two arm-lengths.
And you should order a strong young man to push down on the head of the hip joint while another suspends the patient by their leg; and the other young man pulls up on the hip with force, as this will quickly return it to its proper place.
But in the proper reduction when it is dislocated to the back, the patient should lie on their back with their leg bent as we have recommended earlier, and the strap should be tightly pressed against the dislocated hip and their foot.
And two beams should be placed, one under each of the patient's feet, and the other under their head; and they should be adjusted so that a mat of dampened cloth is wrapped around the middle beam so that the patient will not be hurt by it being between their thighs, as otherwise the extension downward will be painful.
And each person should extend their part, and the doctor should press down on the joint with his hand to guide the reduction according to the art.
And when the dislocation is to the front, then the leg should be extended slightly, and it is according to this narrative instruction that you press your right palm over the patient's armpit, and another person pulls upward on their foot while pressing downward on their shoulder; and with this extension, they will be pulled to the front toward the armpit.
And if the dislocation is to the back, then it should not be extended downward, but it should be raised up from the ground and placed on a hard surface; and if the hip joint is dislocated to the front, as we have said about its placement over two surfaces, then it will be elevated on its side with its leg extended, and its head will be turned back toward its chest, and the straps will be extended according to what we have said earlier.
And it should receive compression with a board over the joint on which side the dislocation is located.
So when the reduction is complete according to what you see — and the sign of the reduction of it is not hidden from you, and it extends its leg with both feet, and when you see its joints bent and the dislocation expanded while the patient makes excuses, then you will know that the part has returned — and then you adjust both sides and press down on the hip joint and extend it with a band so that the hip is not bent in either direction; and let the patient rest for three or four days.
And then untie the bandage and the plaster, and bring one leg up to meet the other.
Then, if you see those wounds healing naturally, then know that the dislocation is cured and let the patient leave.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and bandage that, and stretch that according to your first stretching, and let that be for three days.
Then untie that, and let that delay walking for some days, until it is comforted.
Chapter 32.
Of the cure for a dislocation of the jaw.
The jaw is dislocated in three ways: it is dislocated at the forward parts, and at the inward parts, and at the lower parts to the backward parts; and at the forward parts it is not dislocated.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the patient to apply his jaw to his thigh; then you know that the jaw is dislocated.
And the restoration of all of its manners is that you force the patient to lie down straight, so that he extends his thigh, if he can over it; adhere your two hands to his face, and place his foot between your feet; and then adhere his jaw with your palms, and hold those between your fingers over his jaw with strength, and another extend his foot while the jaw returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that his thigh adheres to the thigh with an easy, non-laborious elevation.
Then bandage that and make the thigh adhere to the thigh, and then tie those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then untie those.
And let the patient not use walking for some days, until he is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of it to you is excused, then administer strong extension with bandages from which in the cure of the hip a preceding narrative was given.
Chapter 33.
Of the cure for a dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle is sometimes dislocated by a small removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to the complement; and its dislocation is made either at the inward parts or at the outward parts.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding to the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure for the removal of it is a gentle reduction, and that it be extended with ease with the hands and be poulticed until it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to the complement, you see that the patient lies down straight, and that he holds a strong and valiant minister of the part behind his back at the middle place of him.
And then you with your right hand hold the highest foot, and with your left hand of the lower part of the foot at the place of the ankle.
And then pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then with the left twice, and then extend it with the left; and press the foot of the leg with the right over the thigh six times.
And do that two times according to what we have counted.
And then press the foot of the leg to the thigh the third time, and you are at the ankle.
So if it returns in one time or two according to this manner, and if you see the foot swollen, that is good; and if not, then repeat the operation on him, for he will return.
But if to you is prohibited the reduction of it with that which we have recommended, then let the patient lie on his back on the ground, and stretch a rope tightly in the ground, and that cord between his thighs, and you already have wrapped a cloth over him so that he does not slide.
And then let the minister of it hold his thighs, and then another minister extend the foot with their hands or with bandages that lie over the nape of the neck; and then extend each minister against the extension of his pair, and let the cord be extended between the thighs of him holding that, so that the body of him is not drawn to the lower parts beyond its extension.
And then let the doctor poultice the dislocation with their hands, and another minister hold the healthy thigh at the lower parts, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And so when the dislocation returns and is declared to you the health of the reduction of it, then pause bandage and stretch that with strong bandages, and tie the leg to the ankle.
And see that you are wary or guard the nerves which are behind over the ankle, lest the bandage over it be violent and lead that strongly.
And then let that be for two days or three; and if the bandage is softened, stretch that.
And then untie that on the third day or the fourth, and let the patient not walk for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk before that space, it is not made certain that the dislocation is not dissolved and ruined, and then let that not receive a cure.
And if pus occurs to him, then let you minister to him in the healing of it that which in our preceding narrative we spoke of, in another place of cure and of poulticing, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
Of the cure for a dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Let that which is dislocated be swollen with little extension six times; and that which is difficult, let it be gentle.
But if the dislocation is in some knuckles of the nape of the neck, then let the patient lie his foot on the ground or on a table, and be standing like that as if he was walking.
And then you stand on your feet and place your foot over that which protrudes from the joints, and then press it with your foot with strength while it returns, and see that it is swollen and does not protrude at the place.
And then place under the nape of his neck a table which covers all the foot, to which are two caps, and then stretch that with decent and firm extension for three days; and then untie that and let him rest from walking for several days, until it is stretched out and its reduction is assured.
Chapter 35.
Of the species of dislocation that are made with sprain or with fracture or with whatever.
When any cause of those happens to you and you persevere in the cure of it and in its restoration, many times you will succeed death.
And therefore let not that you presume upon the cure of it, but that which is wise in the art, of long experience, merciful and compassionate, tardy, not impatient nor presumptuous; and use in the beginning of medicines that yield pus cauterizations only, and let the patient be at divine judgment, unless to which you hope salvation from perdition, with the alleviation of wickedness, and in which appears some hope. And therefore strive you as soon as possible to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before he develops pus cauterization.
And then if the member returns according as you will, then minister medicine that yields pus cauterization and cures the sprain with that which is convenient for it of the swollen joints.
If there is a dislocation with a fracture and bleeding in the bones of the joints, then you pierce their veins and do to them what we have said about simple diseases that precede those diseases in those locations.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that instrument in the ways of fear according to what has been preceded by my preaching; for it is better for you to give praise to your Creator, and more permanent for your glory, and healthier for your true body those things in your time. Thus ends the words of Albucasim perfectly, of which God be blessed. Amen.
